	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikivoyage</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r3736234">.mw-parser-output .oo-ui-icon-unesco{background-image:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/WorldHeritageBlanc.svg")}.mw-parser-output .oo-ui-icon-star{background-image:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg")}.mw-parser-output .oo-ui-icon-ftt{background-image:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Writing_Circle.svg/25px-Writing_Circle.svg.png")}.mw-parser-output .oo-ui-icon-dotm,.mw-parser-output .oo-ui-icon-otbp{background-image:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Yes_Check_Circle.svg/25px-Yes_Check_Circle.svg.png")}</style>
<p><span class="countryData" data-country-calling-code="+1" data-local-dialing-code="" data-currency="USD, $, ¢" style="display:none;"></span>
</p>
<div class="dablink" style="font-style: italic; margin-left: 2em;">"Usa" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Usa_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Usa (disambiguation)">Usa (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p>The <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.visittheusa.com/">United States of America</a></b> is a vast country in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. It borders on <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> to the north and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> to the south, and has a land area of about 9.6 million km<sup>2</sup> (about half the size of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and about the same size as <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>). It also has the world's third-largest population, with more than 320 million people. It includes densely populated cities with sprawling suburbs and vast uninhabited areas of natural beauty. Representing the world's single largest economy with its history of mass immigration dating from the 17th century, it is a "melting pot" of cultures from around the world.
</p><p>Regarded as the most powerful and influential country in the world, it plays a dominant role in the world's cultural landscape, and is famous for its wide array of popular tourist destinations, ranging from the skyscrapers of <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, to the natural wonders of <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Yellowstone">Yellowstone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, to the canyonlands of the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_(United_States_of_America)" title="Southwest (United States of America)">Southwest</a>, to the warm, sunny beaches of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">Southern California</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The United States comprises 50 <b>states</b> and the nation's capital city of <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, which for the purposes of Wikivoyage are grouped into the following regions:
</p>
<div id="region_list"><span style="float: right; text-align:left"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:477px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map-USA-Regions02.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Map-USA-Regions02.png/475px-Map-USA-Regions02.png" decoding="async" width="475" height="493" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Map-USA-Regions02.png/713px-Map-USA-Regions02.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Map-USA-Regions02.png/950px-Map-USA-Regions02.png 2x" data-file-width="2506" data-file-height="2600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map-USA-Regions02.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the United States</div></div></div></span>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#c68d98;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>)<br />Home to gabled churches, rustic antiques, and steeped in American history, New England offers beaches, spectacular seafood, rugged mountains, frequent winter snows, and some of the nation's oldest cities, in a territory small enough to tour (hastily) in a week.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#a9a567;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic" title="Mid-Atlantic">Mid-Atlantic</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>)<br />Ranging from New York to Washington, D.C., the Mid-Atlantic is home to some of the nation's most densely populated cities, historic sites, rolling mountains and seaside resorts.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#c39dc3;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/South_(United_States_of_America)" title="South (United States of America)">South</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(state)" title="Georgia (state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>)<br />The South is celebrated for its hospitality, down-home cooking, and its blues, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock 'n' roll</a>, bluegrass and country music traditions.  This lush, largely subtropical region includes cool, verdant mountains, plantations, and vast cypress swamps.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#ffd0d0;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a></b> <br /><a href="/wiki/North_Florida" title="North Florida">Northern Florida</a> is similar to the rest of the South, but this is not so in the resorts of <a href="/wiki/Orlando" title="Orlando">Orlando</a>, retirement communities, tropical Caribbean-influenced <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Everglades_National_Park" title="Everglades National Park">Everglades</a>, and 1,200&#160;mi (1,900&#160;km) of sandy beaches.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#4da9c4;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Midwest" title="Midwest">Midwest</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>)<br />A region of simple and hospitable people, farmland, forests, picturesque towns, industrial cities and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> — the largest system of freshwater lakes in the world, which forms the <i>North Coast</i> of the U.S.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#80a8ae;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a></b> <br />The second biggest state is like a separate country (and indeed once was), with strong cultural influences from its Spanish and Mexican past. The terrain is quite varied, with swamplands in the southeast, flat land and cotton farms in the South Plains, sandy beaches in South Texas and mountains and <a href="/wiki/Deserts" title="Deserts">deserts</a> in far West Texas.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#d5dc76;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>)<br />A former Wild West frontier land often described as "flatter than a pancake," this region used to consist of endless grasslands. Much of it is now one huge farm after another, with occasional towns, but the remaining prairies are still vast and somewhat desolate.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#80bb89;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains_(United_States_of_America)" title="Rocky Mountains (United States of America)">Rocky Mountains</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>)<br />The spectacular snow-covered <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains_(United_States_of_America)" title="Rocky Mountains (United States of America)">Rockies</a> offer hiking, rafting, excellent skiing, deserts, and some large cities.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#d7a35a;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Southwest_(United_States_of_America)" title="Southwest (United States of America)">Southwest</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>)<br />Heavily influenced by Spanish, Mexican and Native American cultures, this area is home to some of the nation's most spectacular natural attractions and flourishing artistic communities.  Although mostly empty, the region's deserts contain some big cities.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#d56d76;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></b> <br />Like the Southwest, California is heavily influenced by its former Spanish and Mexican rulers, and also by Asian culture and cuisine. California offers world-famous cities, deserts, rainforests, snowy mountains, and beautiful beaches.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#9791ad;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>)<br />The pleasantly mild Pacific Northwest offers outdoor pursuits and cosmopolitan cities.  The terrain features spectacular rainforests, scenic mountains and volcanoes, beautiful coastlines and sage-covered steppes and deserts.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#578e86;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a></b> <br />One fifth as large as the rest of the United States, Alaska reaches well into the Arctic, and features mountainous wilderness, including North America's tallest mountain, <a href="/wiki/Denali" class="mw-redirect" title="Denali">Denali</a>, and Native Alaskan culture unseen elsewhere in the United States.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table style="border-collapse: separate; border-spacing: 2px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:32px;background-color:#4f93c0;">&#160;</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a></b> <br />A volcanic archipelago in the tropical Pacific, 2,300&#160;mi (3,700&#160;km) southwest of California (the nearest state), laid-back Hawaii is a vacation paradise.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>The U.S. also administers a collection of non-state <b>territories</b> around the world, principally in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a></b> and the <b><a href="/wiki/U.S._Virgin_Islands" title="U.S. Virgin Islands">U.S. Virgin Islands</a></b>) and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a></b>, <b><a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a></b>, the <b><a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a></b>, and various uninhabited islands and island groups). As these are quite different from the 50 states from a traveler's point of view, they are covered in separate articles.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cities">Cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG/250px-WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG/375px-WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG/500px-WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1614" data-file-height="1196" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White House south façade, Washington, D.C.</div></div></div>
<p>The United States has over 10,000 cities, towns, and villages. The following is a list of nine of the most notable. Other cities can be found in their corresponding <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_America#Regions" title="United States of America">regions</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">38.904</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-77.016</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/38.904/-77.016/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="38.904" data-lon="-77.016" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">1</a>&#32;</span><span id="Washington,_D.C." class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></b></span></span>&#160;— the nation's capital, filled with major museums and monuments</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">42.358</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-71.063</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/42.358/-71.063/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="42.358" data-lon="-71.063" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">2</a>&#32;</span><span id="Boston" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a></b></span></span>&#160;— best known for its colonial history, its passion for sports, and its universities</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">41.836</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-87.684</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/41.836/-87.684/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="41.836" data-lon="-87.684" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">3</a>&#32;</span><span id="Chicago" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a></b></span></span>&#160;— heart of the Midwest, transportation hub of the nation, and the world's largest commodities trading hub, with massive skyscrapers and other architectural gems</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">34.05</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-118.25</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/34.05/-118.25/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="34.05" data-lon="-118.25" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">4</a>&#32;</span><span id="Los_Angeles" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a></b></span></span>&#160;— home of the film industry, musical artists and surfers, with beautiful mild weather, great natural beauty from mountains to beaches, and endless stretches of freeways</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">25.775</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-80.208</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/25.775/-80.208/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="25.775" data-lon="-80.208" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">5</a>&#32;</span><span id="Miami" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a></b></span></span>&#160;— this city with a vibrant Latin-influenced Caribbean culture attracts sun-seeking Northerners</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">29.95</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-90.066</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/29.95/-90.066/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="29.95" data-lon="-90.066" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">6</a>&#32;</span><span id="New_Orleans" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a></b></span></span>&#160;— "The Big Easy", the birthplace of jazz, is known for its quaint French Quarter, distinctive cuisine and annual Mardi Gras celebration</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">40.712</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-74.005</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/40.712/-74.005/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="40.712" data-lon="-74.005" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">7</a>&#32;</span><span id="New_York_City" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a></b></span></span>&#160;— the country's biggest city, home to Wall Street, big media and advertising, world-class cuisine, arts, architecture, and shopping</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">37.783</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-122.416</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/37.783/-122.416/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="37.783" data-lon="-122.416" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">8</a>&#32;</span><span id="San_Francisco" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a></b></span></span>&#160;— the City by the Bay, featuring the Golden Gate Bridge, vibrant urban neighborhoods, dramatic fog and high technology</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">47.609</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-122.333</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;city&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/47.609/-122.333/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="47.609" data-lon="-122.333" style="background: #0000FF;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">9</a>&#32;</span><span id="Seattle" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a></b></span></span>&#160;— rich museums, monuments, recreation and the Space Needle</li></ul>

<p>These are some of the largest and most famous destinations outside of major cities.
</p>
<ul><li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">63.069</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-151.007</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/63.069/-151.007/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="63.069" data-lon="-151.007" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">1</a>&#32;</span><span id="Denali_National_Park" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Denali_National_Park" title="Denali National Park">Denali National Park</a></b></span></span>&#160;— a remote national park featuring North America's highest peak, Denali (formerly Mt. McKinley)</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">36.3</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-112.6</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/36.3/-112.6/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="36.3" data-lon="-112.6" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">2</a>&#32;</span><span id="Grand_Canyon" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a></b></span></span>&#160;— the world's longest and most visited canyon</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">35.562</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-83.498</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/35.562/-83.498/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="35.562" data-lon="-83.498" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">3</a>&#32;</span><span id="Great_Smoky_Mountains_National_Park" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Great_Smoky_Mountains_National_Park" title="Great Smoky Mountains National Park">Great Smoky Mountains National Park</a></b></span></span>&#160;— national park in the southern Appalachians</li>

<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">43.079</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-79.074</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/43.079/-79.074/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="43.079" data-lon="-79.074" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">6</a>&#32;</span><span id="Niagara_Falls" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls_(New_York)" title="Niagara Falls (New York)">Niagara Falls</a></b></span></span> — the massive waterfalls straddling the border with Canada</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">28.385</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-81.563</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/28.385/-81.563/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="28.385" data-lon="-81.563" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">7</a>&#32;</span><span id="Walt_Disney_World" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_World" title="Walt Disney World">Walt Disney World</a></b></span></span>&#160;— the most popular vacation resort destination in the world</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">44.6</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-110.5</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/44.6/-110.5/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="44.6" data-lon="-110.5" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">8</a>&#32;</span><span id="Yellowstone_National_Park" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a></b></span></span>&#160;— the first national park in the U.S., and home of the Old Faithful geyser</li>
<li><span class="vcard"><span class="noprint listing-coordinates" style="display:none"><span class="geo"><abbr class="latitude">37.85</abbr><abbr class="longitude">-119.55</abbr></span></span><span title="Map for this &#39;vicinity&#39; marker"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink mw-kartographer-autostyled" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/17/37.85/-119.55/en" data-zoom="17" data-lat="37.85" data-lon="-119.55" style="background: #800000;" data-overlays="[&quot;mask&quot;,&quot;around&quot;,&quot;buy&quot;,&quot;city&quot;,&quot;do&quot;,&quot;drink&quot;,&quot;eat&quot;,&quot;go&quot;,&quot;listing&quot;,&quot;other&quot;,&quot;see&quot;,&quot;sleep&quot;,&quot;vicinity&quot;,&quot;view&quot;,&quot;black&quot;,&quot;blue&quot;,&quot;brown&quot;,&quot;chocolate&quot;,&quot;forestgreen&quot;,&quot;gold&quot;,&quot;gray&quot;,&quot;grey&quot;,&quot;lime&quot;,&quot;magenta&quot;,&quot;maroon&quot;,&quot;mediumaquamarine&quot;,&quot;navy&quot;,&quot;red&quot;,&quot;royalblue&quot;,&quot;silver&quot;,&quot;steelblue&quot;,&quot;teal&quot;,&quot;fuchsia&quot;]">9</a>&#32;</span><span id="Yosemite_National_Park" class="fn org listing-name"><b><a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite National Park</a></b></span></span>&#160;—  home of El Capitan, Half Dome, and famous Giant Sequoia trees</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Understand">Understand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Understand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r3768964">@media all and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .wv-quickbar{clear:none!important;float:none!important;margin:0!important;width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .wv-quickbar table{font-size:85%;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #ccc;width:250px}.mw-parser-output .wv-quickbar .info{vertical-align:top;padding:0 5px;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .wv-quickbar .footer{margin-top:5px;border-top:1px solid #ccc;text-align:right}</style><div id="quickbar" class="wv-quickbar floatright">
<table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<p>The United States (also known as the "USA," the "U.S.," or just "America") is not the America of television and movies. It is large, complex, and diverse, with distinct regional identities. Due to the distances involved, travelling between regions can be time-consuming and expensive.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg/220px-Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg/330px-Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg/440px-Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Rock_Mountain_View.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(state)" title="Georgia (state)">Georgia</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <b>contiguous United States</b> or "Lower 48" refers to the territory excluding Alaska and Hawaii. Much of the population lives on the Atlantic, Pacific or Gulf of Mexico coasts or along the Great Lakes. Its only land borders — both quite long — are shared with <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> to the north and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> to the south. The <b>continental United States</b> are the 48 contiguous states, D.C. and Alaska, but exclude Hawaii.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barns_grand_tetons.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Barns_grand_tetons.jpg/220px-Barns_grand_tetons.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Barns_grand_tetons.jpg/330px-Barns_grand_tetons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Barns_grand_tetons.jpg/440px-Barns_grand_tetons.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1195" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barns_grand_tetons.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Grand_Teton_National_Park" title="Grand Teton National Park">Grand Tetons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains_(United_States_of_America)" title="Rocky Mountains (United States of America)">Rocky Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a></div></div></div>
<p>The country has three major mountain ranges. The <b><a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachians</a></b> extend from Canada to <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, a few hundred miles west of the Atlantic Ocean.  They are the oldest of the three ranges and offer spectacular sightseeing and excellent camping spots. The <b><a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains_(United_States_of_America)" title="Rocky Mountains (United States of America)">Rockies</a></b> are, on average, the highest in North America, extending from Alaska to <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, with many areas designated as <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Parks" title="United States National Parks">national parks</a> that offer hiking, camping, skiing, and sightseeing opportunities. The combined <b><a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada" title="Sierra Nevada">Sierra Nevada</a></b> and <b><a href="/wiki/Cascade_Mountains" title="Cascade Mountains">Cascade</a></b> ranges are the youngest. The Sierras extend across the "backbone" of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, with sites such as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tahoe" title="Lake Tahoe">Lake Tahoe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite National Park</a>, then give way to the even younger volcanic Cascade range, with some of the highest points in the country.
</p><p>In the center of the country is the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a>, which includes the entirety of <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, and portions of the surrounding states. This region is characterized by long stretches of flat land, and areas of gentle rolling hills. It consists largely of farmland and prairie.
</p><p>The Gulf of Mexico is to the southeast of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, to the south of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>'s Panhandle, and constitutes the West Coast of Florida.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> define much of the border between the eastern United States and Canada. More fresh water inland seas than lakes, they were formed by the pressure of retreating glaciers at the end of the last Ice Age.  The five lakes span hundreds of miles, bordering the states of <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, and their shores vary from pristine wilderness areas to industrial "rust belt" cities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although much of the U.S. has a <b>temperate</b> climate, there is also a wide variety from Arctic tundra in Alaska to the tropical weather in Hawaii and <a href="/wiki/South_Florida" title="South Florida">South Florida</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> are dry, flat and grassy, turning into <b>desert</b> in the far West and <b>Mediterranean</b> along the California coast.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Winter_in_North_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter in North America">winter</a>, major cities in the North and Midwest can see as much as 2&#160;ft (0.61&#160;m) of snowfall in one day, with cold temperatures.  Summers are humid, but mild. Temperatures over 100&#160;°F (38&#160;°C) sometimes invade the  Midwest and Great Plains. Some areas in the northern plains can experience cold temperatures of −30&#160;°F (−34&#160;°C) during the winter. Temperatures below 0&#160;°F (−18&#160;°C) sometimes reach as far south as <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>.
</p><p>The climate of the <a href="/wiki/South_(United_States_of_America)" title="South (United States of America)">South</a> also varies. In the summer, it is hot and humid, but from October through April the weather can range from 60&#160;°F (16&#160;°C) to short cold spells of 20&#160;°F (−7&#160;°C) or so.
</p><p>The Great Plains and Midwestern states also experience <b><a href="/wiki/Tornadoes" class="mw-redirect" title="Tornadoes">tornadoes</a></b> from the late spring to early fall, earlier in the south and later in the north. States along the Atlantic coast and the Gulf of Mexico may experience <b><a href="/wiki/Hurricanes" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurricanes">hurricanes</a></b> between June and November.  These intense and dangerous storms frequently miss the U.S. mainland, but evacuations are often ordered and should be heeded.
</p><p>The Rockies are cold and snowy. Some parts of the Rockies see over 500 inches (1,300&#160;cm) of snow in a season. Even during the summer, temperatures are cool in the mountains, and snow can fall nearly year-round. It is dangerous to go up in the mountains unprepared in the winter and the roads through them can get very icy.
</p><p>The deserts of the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_(United_States_of_America)" title="Southwest (United States of America)">Southwest</a> are hot and dry during the summer, with temperatures often exceeding 100&#160;°F (38&#160;°C). Thunderstorms can be expected in the southwest frequently from July through September. Winters are mild, and snow is unusual. Average annual precipitation is low, usually less than 10&#160;in (250&#160;mm).
</p><p>Cool and damp weather is common much of the year in the coastal northwest (Oregon and Washington west of the Cascade Range, and the northern part of California west of the Coast Ranges/Cascades). Summers (July through September) are usually quite dry with low humidity, though, making it the ideal climate for outdoor activities. Rain is most frequent in winter, snow is rare, especially along the coast, and extreme temperatures are uncommon. Rain falls almost exclusively from late fall through early spring along the coast. East of the Cascades, the northwest is considerably drier. Much of the inland northwest is either semi-arid or desert, especially in Oregon.
</p><p>Northeastern and Upper Southern cities are known for summers with temperatures reaching into the 90s °F (32&#160;°C) or more, with extremely high humidity, usually over 80%.  This can be a drastic change from the Southwest. High humidity means that the temperature can feel hotter than actual readings. The Northeast also experiences snow, and at least once every few years there will be a dumping of the white stuff in enormous quantities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td>United States historical travel topics:<br /><b><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_cultures_of_North_America" title="Indigenous cultures of North America">Indigenous nations</a> → <a href="/wiki/Early_United_States_history" title="Early United States history">Pre-Civil War</a> → <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> → <a href="/wiki/Old_West" title="Old West">Old West</a> → <a href="/wiki/Industrialization_of_the_United_States" title="Industrialization of the United States">Industrialization</a> → <a href="/wiki/Postwar_United_States" title="Postwar United States">Postwar</a></b>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Native Americans</b>, or <b>American Indians</b>, arrived 13,500 to 16,000 years ago from migrating northeast Asian peoples crossing the Bering Strait into <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, and represented a wide variety of sophisticated societies that existed before the first arrival of Europeans in the late 15th century. The Mississippian cultures built huge settlements across the Southeast, and the Anasazi built elaborate cliff-side towns in the Southwest. These societies were decimated by Old-World diseases such as smallpox and were pushed west by warfare and encroaching European settlers. Their diminished numbers led to further marginalization, although today their cultures endure and continue to contribute to the American experience.
</p><p><b>European colonization</b> began in the 16th and 17th centuries. <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> gained large holdings; the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> also established outposts. The first English colonies, founded in <a href="/wiki/Jamestown_(Virginia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamestown (Virginia)">Jamestown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> (1607) and <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_(Massachusetts)" title="Plymouth (Massachusetts)">Plymouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> (1620), formed the kernel of what is now known as the United States.
</p><p>In the North, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> was settled by religious immigrants — <b>Puritans</b> — who later spread and founded most of the other <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> colonies, creating a highly religious and idealistic region. Other religious groups also founded colonies, including the Quakers in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and Roman Catholics in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>. The Middle Colonies of <a href="/wiki/New_York_State" class="mw-redirect" title="New York State">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> became the North's cosmopolitan center.
</p><p>Longer growing seasons in the Southern colonies, which remained dominated by Virginia, gave them richer agricultural prospects, especially for cotton and tobacco. As in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, indentured servants, convicts and later <b><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">African slaves</a></b> were imported and forced to cultivate large plantations. Slavery was practiced in both North and South, but its greater importance to the South's economy eventually caused tremendous upheaval.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg/250px-Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg/375px-Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg/500px-Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Independence_hall_1_bs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Independence Hall in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> was where the Declaration of Independence and the Constitution were drafted</div></div></div>
<p>By the early 18th century, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Great Britain</a> had colonized the Atlantic coast from <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(state)" title="Georgia (state)">Georgia</a> north into what is now Canada. The last major British migration to the territory that would become the United States took place in middle decades of that century when the Appalachia region was settled. In 1763, British dominance in North America was established after the global Seven Years' War. In part to finance the North American campaigns of the war, known as the French and Indian War, Britain imposed unpopular taxes and regulations on its colonists. This precipitated revolution in 1775 and on 4 July 1776, colonists from 13 colonies declared independence. The <b>Revolutionary War</b> lasted until 1783, when the new United States of America gained sovereignty over all British land between the Atlantic and the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. Those still loyal to the British mostly fled north to what is today <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, which continued to remain under British rule.
</p><p>Wrangling over the formation of a national government lasted until 1787 when a constitution was agreed upon. Its Enlightenment-era ideas about individual liberty have since inspired the founding decrees of many states. <b>George Washington</b>, the general-in-chief of the revolutionary army, was elected the first president. By the turn of the 19th century, the newly-built <b><a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></b> was established as the national capital.
</p><p>New states were created as white settlers moved west beyond the Appalachian Mountains. The Native American populations were displaced and further harrowed by war and disease. The 1803 <b>Louisiana Purchase</b> of French lands to the west of the Mississippi (charted by the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Trail" title="Lewis and Clark Trail">Lewis and Clark expedition</a>) effectively doubled the size of the nation, and provided "Indian Territory" in what is now Oklahoma for the many Native American tribes from the east that were forcibly relocated during the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" class="mw-redirect" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a> of the 1830s.
</p><p>Further disagreements with British commerce policies arising from the Napoleonic Wars and Royal Navy impressment led to the <b>War of 1812</b>. There were over two years of dramatic action on land and sea that included an attempted invasion of Canada and the burning of the White House and public buildings in Washington, D.C. The final stalemate saw virtually no changes of territory, but the war galvanized separate American and Canadian identities. The national anthem, "The Star-Spangled Banner", was conceived during this war. Western Native American tribes that had sided with the British suffered greatly as their territory was given to white settlers. 
</p><p>After the war, industry and infrastructure were expanded greatly, particularly in the Northeast; see <a href="/wiki/American_Industry_Tour" title="American Industry Tour">American Industry Tour</a>. Roads and canals came first and helped people spread inland. In 1825, the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a> connected the Atlantic to the Great Lakes. By the late 1860s, railroads and telegraph lines connected the east and west coasts via the industrial hub of <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Midwest" title="Midwest">Midwest</a>. In the early 19th century, a series of religious revivals, the Second Great Awakening, led to various reform movements that strove for goals such as temperance, the abolition of slavery, and women's suffrage.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg/250px-Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg/375px-Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg/500px-Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alamo_San_Antonio_Texas_USA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1836 Battle of the Alamo (<a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>) was a crucial moment of the Texas Revolution.</div></div></div>
<p>U.S. expansion south and west chipped away at Spanish and Mexican territory. Spain sold <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> in 1813 after American military intervention, and an 1836 rebellion by American settlers in Mexican Texas founded an independent republic which was absorbed into the Union ten years later. This sparked the <b>Mexican–American War</b> in which Mexico lost what is now <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, and the contiguous United States essentially assumed its modern outline. Native Americans were relegated to reservations and continued to be purged by treaty, military force, and disease from settlers on the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Trail" title="Oregon Trail">Oregon Trail</a> and other westward routes. (See also "<a href="/wiki/Old_West" title="Old West">Old West</a>".)
</p><p>Federal governance was light and the states were highly autonomous. By the 1850s, there was irreconcilable disparity between the industrialized and more urban Northern states, which had all outlawed slavery within three decades of the revolution, and the plantation-dependent rural South. Many in the North wanted to impose a national ban on the expansion of slavery, while the Southern states sought to expand slavery into new territories. Abolitionists operated an <a href="/wiki/Underground_Railroad" title="Underground Railroad">Underground Railroad</a> leading fugitive slaves in the northern states to freedom in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. In 1861, eleven Southern states, fearful of marginalization and the avowedly anti-slavery President <b>Abraham Lincoln</b>, broke from the Union and formed an independent Confederate States of America. The ensuing <b><a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a></b> remains the bloodiest conflict on American soil and killed hundreds of thousands of people. In 1865, Union forces prevailed, firmly cementing the federal government's authority over the states. Slavery was abolished nationwide and the Confederate states were re-admitted into the Union during a period of Reconstruction. The former slaves and their descendants were to remain an economic and social underclass, particularly in the South.
</p><p>Russia sold its tenuously held Alaskan territory in 1867, and independent Hawaii was annexed in 1898. The United States' decisive victory over Spain in the 1898 <b>Spanish–American War</b> gained it colonial territories: <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> (granted independence a few years later), the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> (granted independence shortly after <a href="/wiki/Pacific_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific war">World War II</a>), <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a> (which remain American dependencies). The boundaries of the United States took the form we know today in 1959, when the territories of Alaska and Hawaii were granted statehood.
</p><p>In the late 19th and into the 20th century, Southern and Eastern Europeans, Russian Jews and Irish bolstered the continuing industrialization of the eastern cities by providing cheap labor. Many Southern African-Americans fled rural poverty and racism for industrial jobs in the North. Other immigrants, including many Scandinavians and Germans, moved to newly opened territories in the West and Midwest, where land was given to anyone who would develop it.
</p><p>The United States' entrance into <b><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a></b> in 1917 marked the start of an era in which it would become a world power. However, soon after the victory the U.S. eschewed international involvement and refused to join the nascent League of Nations, effectively crippling the organization. Real wealth grew rapidly and in the Roaring 20s stock speculation created an immense financial "bubble".  It burst in 1929, leading to the global economic havoc of the <b>Great Depression</b>. The resulting privation fostered a culture of sacrifice and hard work that would serve the country well in the coming conflict.  It also ushered in President Franklin D. Roosevelt. His "New Deal" was a series of government programs that constructed thousands of buildings and bridges across the country while creating the basis of the American welfare state.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG/250px-USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG/375px-USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG/500px-USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Arizona_Memorial.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The USS Arizona Memorial at <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" class="mw-redirect" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1941 Japan attacked <b>Pearl Harbor</b>, a Hawaiian naval base, plunging the United States into <b>World War II</b> on the side of the Allied Powers - see <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_in_Europe" title="World War II in Europe">World War II in Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a>. The U.S. developed atomic bombs and dropped them twice on Japan in 1945, abruptly ending the war. By the end of the war, the United States had firmly established itself as the world's dominant economic power, responsible for nearly half of global industrial production.  During the ensuing <b><a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a></b>, the United States and the Soviet Union jostled for power while courting their own mutually assured destruction with nuclear weapons. Although war between the two superpowers never occurred, both sides were indirectly involved in covert operations and military endeavors through various proxy states that continue to (often negatively) affect the view people have of the United States and its role in global politics.
</p><p>For the century after the Civil War, black people, though ostensibly equal citizens under the post–Civil War amendments to the U.S. Constitution, suffered through strong social, economic, and political discrimination and state-sanctioned segregation, especially in the South. A movement fighting for full civil rights for black Americans gained strength following World War II, when returning black veterans who fought against racism abroad came home to find they were still heavily discriminated against. The <b>civil rights movement</b> vehemently, but largely peacefully, vied for equal rights. With <b>Martin Luther King, Jr.</b>, a charismatic preacher, as its most visible leader, the movement came to a head in 1963 when 200,000–300,000 people flooded the capital to listen to him. The landmark Civil Rights Act that was passed in 1964 outlawed discrimination based on race, color, religion, sex, or national origin, although such discrimination does still exist, mostly in less blatant forms. It would not be until the election of Barack Obama 44 years later in 2008 that the country would have its first African-American president. A revived women's movement in the 1960s also led to wide-ranging changes in American society.
</p><p>Postwar America was characterized by affluence and industrialization. People left agriculture and moved to the cities to become part of an increasingly technology-based economy. American car culture emerged in the 1950s and was supported by the construction of a comprehensive Interstate Highway System. These trends also led to the rise of suburbia and a decline in public transportation and rail travel, making touring the <a href="/wiki/United_States_without_a_car" title="United States without a car">United States without a car</a> particularly difficult to this day. They also resulted in <b>white flight</b> to the suburbs in many American cities, leaving many black people behind in blighted inner city neighborhoods. The American consumer culture, Hollywood movies and many forms of popular music established the United States as a cultural superpower in the world. The U.S. grew into one of the world's major centers of higher education, and is now home to many of the world's most prestigious universities, attracting more international students than any other country.
</p><p>Since the collapse of the Soviet Union, the United States has emerged as the world's sole superpower, and while its hegemony is increasingly being challenged by a resurgent <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, it continues to play the dominant military, economic, political, and cultural role in world affairs. Hopes that after the fall of America's chief rival, expensive and sometimes disastrous wars (such as the Vietnam War) were a thing of the past sadly haven't proven true. Administrations since the end of the Cold War have all had to deal in one form or other with what they call the threat of "rogue states", terrorism, and a rapidly changing global political landscape. The terrorist attacks of September 11, 2001, are still very much an open wound and influence the political debate to this day, with heightened security measures at airports being just one way in which terrorism (or the fear of it) has affected travelers. Economically, the "Great Recession", triggered in 2007 by the collapse of the housing market bubble, came to an end in 2009, but the average American continues to feel the negative effects several years on.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The United States is a federal republic. Its major constituents are the 50 states and the District of Columbia (Washington D.C.); it also has various island territories in the Caribbean and Pacific that are strongly—but often not fully—integrated into the union.
</p><p>The federal government derives its power from the U.S. Constitution, which is the oldest written constitution in continuous use. Within the overarching federal laws, each state maintains its own constitution, government and laws, and so retains considerable autonomy within the federation. State laws can vary in their details but are, for the most part, fairly uniform from state to state.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Presidents of the United States">President</a> is elected every four years and is the head of the federal government and head of state. He and his administration form the executive branch. The bicameral Congress (comprising the lower House of Representatives and the upper Senate) is also popularly elected, and constitutes the legislative branch. The Supreme Court tops the judicial branch. State governments are organized similarly, with governors, legislatures, and judiciaries.
</p><p>Two major political parties have dominated at state and federal levels since the end of the Civil War: the Republican Party (often referred to as the GOP, short for "Grand Old Party") and the Democratic Party. Since the 1960s the Republican Party has become generally the more right-wing or "conservative" party whereas the Democratic Party is usually the more left-wing or "liberal" of the two parties. While smaller political parties exist, the winner-take-all electoral system means that they rarely succeed at any level. Although in much of the world red and blue indicate left- and right-leaning parties respectively, they are reversed in the U.S so that Republicans are red and Democrats are blue.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bourbon_Street_New_Orleans_Louisiana_USA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Bourbon_Street_New_Orleans_Louisiana_USA.jpg/250px-Bourbon_Street_New_Orleans_Louisiana_USA.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4f/Bourbon_Street_New_Orleans_Louisiana_USA.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bourbon_Street_New_Orleans_Louisiana_USA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The South's famous Bourbon Street, <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a></div></div></div>
<p>The United States is made up of many diverse ethnic groups and the culture varies greatly across the vast area of the country and even within cities&#160;– a city like New York will have dozens, if not hundreds, of different ethnicities represented within a neighborhood. Despite this difference, there exists a strong sense of national identity and certain predominant cultural traits. Generally, Americans tend to believe strongly in personal freedom and responsibility, and that an individual determines his or her own success or failure, but there are many exceptions. You will find Mississippi in the South to be very different culturally from Massachusetts in the North.
</p><p>Religion is very important in the United States with 80% of people identifying with having a religious affiliation. Current estimates are that 49% of Americans belong to a Christian Protestant church and another 23% are Roman Catholic. 5% of Americans belong to non-Christian religions such as Judaism, Islam, Hinduism and Buddhism. Many businesses and institutions are closed on Sundays, and a number of areas in the South and Midwest forbid certain activities to take place on Sundays, while some Jewish businesses close on Friday nights and Saturdays for the Sabbath.
</p><p>Although officially a secular state, the United States is in practice more religious than other Western countries such as <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> countries. However, this trend varies greatly by region, with the West Coast and Northeast being largely secular and the <a href="/wiki/South_(United_States_of_America)" title="South (United States of America)">American South</a> being heavily Evangelical Christian. Differences in religiosity often correlate with politics, so the Northeast, West Coast, Hawaii and Chicago metropolitan area are generally progressive and Democratic; most of the South and heavily Mormon states like Utah, Idaho and Wyoming are very conservative and Republican; and much of the rest of the country (e.g., several Midwestern, Southwestern/Rocky Mountain, and Southern coastal states) is nearly evenly divided between Democrats and Republicans. However, even this can be misleading, as many conservative states have college towns and major cities that are very liberal, while liberal states often have rural areas that are very conservative. A trend of the last few decades is one of increasing geographic political polarization. Most Americans now live in counties where their political inclination has a majority of 20% or greater and cities are - on the whole - becoming more and more liberal while rural areas are becoming more and more conservative with the middle ground disappearing.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holidays">Holidays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Holidays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="pp_infobox" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left: 1em; width:40%;" cellpadding="8" cellspacing="1">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="background-color:#effafa; font-size:90%; border:1px solid #ccc; padding-left: 1em; padding-right: 1em; text-align:left; width:100%;">
<p><b>Always gotta be different</b>
</p><p>Whereas most countries celebrate Labor Day on May 1 to commemorate the Haymarket affair of 1886, the U.S. chose to celebrate it in September, due to fear that a May celebration would encourage similar Haymarket-style protests and energize the radical left.
</p><p>November 11, which is Remembrance Day in Europe and Canada, has been expanded to celebrate all veterans of the U.S. armed forces; Memorial Day serves the purpose of recognizing war dead.
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are no nationwide, mandatory public holidays. Federal holidays are the most centrally coordinated holidays, but they are only mandatory for the federal government and banks. All federal government offices, post offices and banks close on federal holidays, but private businesses may freely choose whether or not to observe them.   
</p><p>Nearly all states and localities observe the federal holidays; most also observe an additional handful of their own. If a federal holiday falls on a weekend, the observance will be shifted to the <b>nearest weekday</b> (either Friday or Monday), with closures similar to Presidents Day in February. The festivities and major retail closings will occur on the annual date, even if it's the weekend.  
</p><p>The time between Thanksgiving (the fourth Thursday in November) and January 1 has such a high concentration of major holidays that it is often simply called "the holiday season."  School and work vacations are commonly taken during this period, with people visiting family and friends. Airports, Interstates, bus stations, and train stations will be very crowded near the major holidays. If you must travel, allow extra time to check in and clear security. This is also a major gift-giving season: most shopping malls and department stores will be crowded, especially on the day after Thanksgiving, the week before Christmas, and the day after Christmas.
</p><p>As in most other countries where alcohol is legal, be careful on the roads at and around major holidays, such as New Year's, Memorial Day, or Independence Day, as there are higher concentrations of drunk drivers on the roads then.
</p><p>In the list below, federal holidays are listed in <i><b>bold italics</b></i>. In addition to federal holidays, the following list includes holidays that may inconvenience travelers, and nationwide celebrations of other events that may be of interest to travellers:
</p>
<ul><li><i><b>New Year's Day</b></i> (1 January)&#160;— most non-retail businesses closed; parades; brunches and football parties.</li>
<li><i><b>Martin Luther King Day</b></i> (third Monday in January)&#160;— many government offices and banks closed; people volunteer in their communities; speeches, especially on African-American history and culture.</li>
