<li><b>Chinese</b> or <b>Lunar New Year</b> (January/February&#160;— varies based on the lunar calendar)&#160;— Chinese-, Korean- and Vietnamese-Americans hold cultural celebrations.</li>
<li><b>Super Bowl Sunday</b> (usu. first Sunday in February)&#160;— This annual championship game of the National Football League (NFL) is the most-watched sporting event of the year; supermarkets, bars, and electronics stores busy; big football-watching parties.</li>
<li><b>(St.) Valentine's Day</b> (14 February)&#160;— private celebration of romance and love. Most restaurants are crowded; finer restaurants may require reservations made well in advance.</li>
<li><i><b>Presidents Day</b></i> (third Monday in February; officially <b>Washington's Birthday</b>)&#160;— many government offices and banks closed; many stores have sales.</li>
<li><b>St. Patrick's Day</b> (17 March)&#160;— Irish-themed parades and parties. Expect bars to be crowded. They will often feature themed drink specials. The wearing of green clothes or accessories is common.</li>
<li><b>Good Friday</b> (the Friday before Easter) — Christian (especially Catholic) religious observances. Some restaurants and shops close. The governments of some states with large Catholic populations (e.g. New Jersey) observe the holiday and close government offices on this day.</li>
<li><b>Easter</b> (a Sunday in March or April)&#160;— Christian religious observances. Many fast-food restaurants are closed, but sit-down restaurants are more likely to be open. Major retailers generally open; smaller shops may or may not close.</li>
<li><b>Passover</b> (varies based on the Jewish calendar, eight days around Easter)&#160;— Jewish religious observances. Many American Jews invite non-Jews to their <i>Seder</i> on one of the first two nights. Expect very heavy traffic on Seder afternoons and evenings in areas with large Jewish populations such as the New York Metro Area and South Florida.</li>
<li><b>Cinco de Mayo</b> (5 May)&#160;— A minor holiday in Mexico that celebrates the 1862 Battle of <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">Puebla</a> against the French, but a major cultural celebration for Mexican-Americans. Expect bars to be crowded, even in places without large Mexican-American communities.</li>
<li><b>Mother's Day</b> (second Sunday in May)&#160;— Children and adults give gifts to their mothers. Most restaurants are crowded, especially for brunch and lunch; finer restaurants may require reservations made well in advance.</li>
<li><i><b>Memorial Day</b></i> (last Monday in May)&#160;— most non-retail businesses closed; some patriotic observances; trips to beaches and parks; traditional beginning of summer tourism season.</li>
<li><b>Father's Day</b> (third Sunday in June)&#160;— Children and adults give gifts to their fathers. Many restaurants and sporting events are crowded, although not to the same extent as Mother's Day.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Fourth_of_July_at_the_U.S._Capitol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>4th of July fireworks in Washington D.C.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i><b>Independence Day/Fourth of July</b></i> (4 July)&#160;— most non-retail businesses closed; patriotic parades and concerts, cookouts and trips to beaches and parks, fireworks at dusk, often continuing well into the night. Almost every town puts on some sort of festivity to celebrate the day.</li>
<li><i><b>Labor Day</b></i> (first Monday in September)&#160;— most non-retail businesses closed; cookouts and trips to beaches and parks; many stores have sales; traditional ending of summer tourism season.</li>
<li><b>Rosh Hashanah</b> and <b>Yom Kippur</b> (varies based on the Jewish calendar, September or early October)&#160;— Jewish religious observances.</li>
<li><i><b>Columbus Day</b></i> (second Monday in October)&#160;— many government offices and banks closed; some stores have sales.  Italian-themed parades in some cities. Columbus Day can be controversial, especially among Native Americans and Latinos, and is not as widely observed as it was in the past. In some places, Columbus Day has been renamed as Indigenous People's Day, with celebrations of tribal cultures occurring.</li>
<li><b>Halloween</b> (31 October)&#160;— Children dress up in costume and go trick-or-treating (knocking on other houses' doors to get candy and other treats). There are spooky attractions, such as haunted corn mazes, hayrides and costume parties. Some small family-owned shops and restaurants may close early in the evening. Adults get in on the action too: boozy Halloween parties and bar-hopping in costume is common.</li>
<li><i><b>Veterans Day</b></i> (11 November)&#160;— government offices and banks closed; some patriotic observances.</li>
<li><i><b>Thanksgiving Day</b></i> (fourth Thursday in November)&#160;— Family dinners with roast turkey as the centerpiece; many people fly or drive to visit extended family. <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> host parades, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> and many other cities hold races. Many other smaller events fill the landscape, including a recreation of the original Thanksgiving dinner in <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_(Massachusetts)" title="Plymouth (Massachusetts)">Plymouth</a>, Massachusetts. Airports are extremely crowded on the Wednesday before and Sunday after Thanksgiving. Most businesses closed, including grocery stores and many restaurants.</li>
<li><b>Black Friday</b> (day after Thanksgiving)&#160;— Major Christmas shopping traditionally begins, most stores have sales and many open in the very early morning (with a few now opening on Thanksgiving night). Most non-retail employees are given Friday off or take it as a holiday.</li>
<li><b>Hanukkah/Chanukah</b> (varies based on the Jewish calendar, eight days usually in December)&#160;— Jewish religious observances, often culturally associated with Christmas.</li>
<li><i><b>Christmas</b></i> (25 December)&#160;— Families and close friends exchange gifts; Christian religious observances. Almost all businesses, grocery stores, and many restaurants closed the evening before and all day. Some government offices close at noon on the 24th, with everything closed on the 25th. However, many Chinese and Jewish businesses remain open.</li>
<li><b>Kwanzaa</b> (26 December&#160;– 1 January)&#160;— African-American cultural observances.</li>
<li><b>New Year's Eve</b> (31 December)&#160;— many restaurants and bars open late; lots of parties, especially in big cities. Some government offices close at noon.</li></ul>
<p>All U.S. embassies are closed on the federal holidays in addition to the holidays of the host country.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Units_of_measure">Units of measure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Units of measure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Metric_and_Imperial_equivalents" title="Metric and Imperial equivalents">Metric and Imperial equivalents</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>The United States is the only industrialized country that eschews the metric system. Instead it uses "customary units" (feet, miles, gallons, pounds, etc.), which are largely derived from the English units of the 18th century, and are sometimes different from the imperial units that occasionally linger in Britain. Road distances are given in miles and speed limits in miles per hour (1 mile is 1.61&#160;km, or 1&#160;km is 0.62 miles). One of the more confusing things is that an "ounce" can be either a measure of weight or (as a "fluid ounce") a measure of volume. The U.S. <i>fluid ounce</i> is also slightly larger than its imperial counterpart, while U.S. gallons, quarts and pints are smaller than their counterparts. Gasoline and other liquids are usually sold per gallon, quart, or fluid ounce (a U.S. gallon is 3.78 liters, so a U.S. quart [a <i>quart</i>er gallon] is slightly less than a liter). Beverages such as soda are sometimes sold by the liter and other times sold by the fluid ounce, with just under 34 ounces to a liter. Temperatures are usually reported in Fahrenheit only; 32° is actually freezing, not warm! Most cars' speedometers show both miles and kilometers per hour (good for trips to Canada and Mexico), and almost all packaged foods and other products are labeled in both systems. Outside of science, medicine and the military, there is little day-to-day exposure to the metric system, so Americans will assume you understand the U.S. customary measures.
</p><p>There is no formal regulation of clothes or shoe sizes, and the only thing you can count on is that sizes tend to be consistent within the same brand. For shoe sizes some trial and error will be needed for <i>each model</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_zones">Time zones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Time zones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US-Timezones.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/US-Timezones.svg/350px-US-Timezones.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/US-Timezones.svg/525px-US-Timezones.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/US-Timezones.svg/700px-US-Timezones.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1238" data-file-height="956" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US-Timezones.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Time zones of the U.S. as of 2007; nowadays, some Indiana counties have moved to Eastern time</div></div></div>
<p>Taking into consideration even the small territories in the Pacific Ocean (some of which cannot be easily accessed) the U.S. spans eleven <a href="/wiki/Time_zones" title="Time zones">time zones</a>. Just four time zones are used in the contiguous 48 states, with an additional two covering Alaska and Hawaii. Time zone borders do not always correspond to state borders.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Eastern Time</b> (UTC-5):  <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> except extreme northwestern counties, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> except the southwest and northwest corners, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware_(state)" class="mw-redirect" title="Delaware (state)">Delaware</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(state)" title="Georgia (state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> except western part of panhandle.</li>
<li><b>Central Time</b> (UTC-6): <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, the southwest and northwest corners of <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, western and middle <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, north and east <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, middle and eastern <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, part of western <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>(panhandle).</li>
<li><b>Mountain Time</b> (UTC-7): southwest <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, western <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, a sliver of <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, a sliver of <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, a corner of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wendover_(Utah/Nevada)" title="Wendover (Utah/Nevada)">West Wendover, Nevada</a></li>
<li><b>Pacific Time</b> (UTC-8):  <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> except West Wendover</li>
<li><b>Alaska Time</b> (UTC-9): <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, except the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a></li>
<li><b>Hawaii-Aleutian Time</b> (UTC-10): <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a></li></ul>
<p>Most parts of the U.S. observe Daylight Saving Time; Hawaii and Arizona (except the Navajo Nation) do not.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Talk">Talk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Talk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="pp_infobox" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left: 1em; width:40%;" cellpadding="8" cellspacing="1">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="background-color:#effafa; font-size:90%; border:1px solid #ccc; padding-left: 1em; padding-right: 1em; text-align:left; width:100%;">
<p><b>"Two countries divided by a common language"</b>
</p><p>Speakers of British English will find many terms which differ in American English. Here are a few:
</p>
<ul><li>chips &#8212; crisps</li>
<li>diaper — nappy</li>
<li>elevator &#8212; lift</li>
<li>highway, freeway, expressway &#8212; motorway</li>
<li>flashlight &#8212; torch</li>
<li>fries — chips</li>
<li>friend, buddy - mate</li>
<li>cookies &#8212; biscuits</li>
<li>biscuits &#8212; scones</li>
<li>gas, gasoline &#8212; petrol</li>
<li>subway &#8212; underground</li>
<li>line &#8212; queue</li>
<li>apartment, apartment building &#8212; flat</li>
<li>liquor store, package store &#8211; off licence, off sales</li>
<li>restroom, bathroom, lavatory &#8211; toilet, loo</li>
<li>round-trip ticket &#8212; return ticket</li>
<li>sidewalk &#8212; footpath or pavement</li>
<li>to-go (in ordering food) &#8212; take-away</li></ul>
<p>See the article <a href="/wiki/English_language_varieties" title="English language varieties">English language varieties</a> for more words that differ across both versions.
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Almost all Americans speak <b>English</b>. Most Americans speak in accents that are recognizably similar to one another and to one traditionally associated with the Midwest, which was popularized in the 20th century by American radio, TV and movies. Although many Americans can discern differences between quite a few accents, the ones most likely to be heard as distinctive by foreign visitors include those commonly spoken in the South and Texas, the Boston area, the New York City area, the upper Midwest and Hawaii.
</p><p>American English differs somewhat from the English spoken in other parts of the English speaking world. These differences are mostly minor, and primarily around minor spelling and pronunciation differences. One important difference, though, is that dates are always written MM/DD/YYYY or MM/DD. See the article on <a href="/wiki/English_language_varieties" title="English language varieties">English language varieties</a> for a detailed discussion.
</p><p>Many African-Americans and some other Americans also speak African-American Vernacular English (AAVE), which has somewhat different grammar and vocabulary from styles of American English usually regarded as standard. Nearly all African-Americans can switch back and forth between AAVE and standard American English effortlessly. Generally, if you are not African-American, you should not try to speak AAVE, even if it's spoken to you; it would be considered odd and possibly offensive. Spanglish — an admixture of Spanish and English — is similarly commonplace in many areas with large Hispanic populations, and code-switching between Spanglish and standard American English is similarly commonplace.
</p><p>Visitors are generally <b>expected to speak and understand English</b>. While many Americans study a foreign language in school (overwhelmingly Spanish followed by French), few progress past the basics. Popular tourist sites often have signs and information available in other languages. Americans have a long history of immigration and are very accommodating towards foreign accents, and will sometimes take the effort to help you by speaking in a more standard accent. 
</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/Spanish_phrasebook" title="Spanish phrasebook">Spanish</a></b> is the first language of Puerto Rico and a large minority of residents on the mainland (with the fifth-largest Spanish speaking population in the world). Spanish speakers in the United States are often Puerto Ricans or first- and second-generation immigrants from Latin America. As a result, the Spanish spoken is almost invariably a Latin American dialect. Spanish is the primary second language in many parts of the United States such as California, the Southwest, Texas, Florida, and the metropolitan areas of Chicago and New York City. Many of these areas have Spanish-language radio and television stations, with local, national and Mexican programs. Most publications from the federal government, and those of some state and municipal governments are available in Spanish. Many establishments and government offices in major commercial and tourist areas have Spanish-speaking staff on duty, and it is possible with some difficulty to get by in the major cities and main tourist attractions speaking only Spanish.
</p><p>You may encounter other languages in some regions, like Hawaiian, French, American Indian languages, Yiddish, and Pennsylvania Dutch. These are covered in regional articles.
</p><p><b>American Sign Language</b> (<b>ASL</b>) is the dominant sign language in the United States. When events are interpreted, they will be interpreted in ASL. Users of French Sign Language and other related languages may find ASL intelligible, as they share much vocabulary, but users of Japanese Sign Language, British Sign Language, or Auslan will not. Closed-captioning on television is widespread, but far from ubiquitous. Many theaters offer FM loops or other assistive listening devices, but captioning and interpreters are rarer.
</p><p>For the blind, many signs and displays include <b>Braille</b> transcriptions of the printed English. Larger restaurant chains, museums, and parks may offer Braille menus and guidebooks, but you'll likely have to ask for them.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Get_in">Get in</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Get in">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div style="display: table; background-color: #faf0ff; border: 1px solid #507; margin: 0.5em 1em 0.5em 1em; padding: 6px;" class="pp_warningbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td style="width: 56px;"><img alt="Travel Warning" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/50px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Travel Warning" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/75px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/100px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="765" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size: 130%; color: #507"><b>Visa restrictions:</b></span>
<div style="font-size: 100%">Due to the 2020 coronavirus outbreak, entry will be denied to foreign nationals who have been to China in the past 14 days, except immediate family of U.S. citizens and permanent residents. U.S. citizens who have been to Hubei in the past 14 days will be quarantined for 14 days upon arrival. Effective February 2, U.S. citizens who have been to other parts of China within 14 days must undergo screening and self-quarantine.
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>The United States has <b>exceptionally onerous and complicated</b> visa requirements. Read up carefully before your visit, especially if you need to apply for a visa, and consult the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://travel.state.gov/visa/temp/types/types_1265.html">Bureau of Consular Affairs</a>. Travellers have been refused entry for many reasons, often trivial.  Since 2019, visitors to the US have also been required to fully document mobile phone numbers, e-mail addresses and on-line identities they've used in the past five years, during the application process.
</p>
<div style="display: table; background-color: #faf0ff; border: 1px solid #507; margin: 0.5em 1em 0.5em 1em; padding: 6px;" class="pp_warningbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td style="width: 56px;"><img alt="Travel Warning" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/50px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Travel Warning" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/75px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/100px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="765" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size: 130%; color: #507"><b>Visa restrictions:</b></span>
<div style="font-size: 100%">An executive order has been signed banning immigration clearance and the issuance of visas for citizens of <b>Eritrea</b>, <b>Iran</b>, <b>Kyrgyzstan</b>, <b>Libya</b>, <b>Myanmar</b>, <b>Nigeria</b>, <b>North Korea</b>, <b>Somalia</b>, <b>Sudan</b>, <b>Syria</b>,  <b>Venezuela</b>, <b>Yemen</b> and <b>Tanzania</b>. Depending on the country, there might be exemptions for people in specific visa classes, though you should still expect extra scrutiny from immigration even if one of the exemptions applies to you. See <a href="/wiki/Avoiding_travel_through_the_United_States" title="Avoiding travel through the United States">Avoiding travel through the United States</a> if you would like to look for alternative routes to non-U.S. destinations. If you are from one of the affected countries and in the U.S. on a long-term visa (e.g. work, student or immigrant visa), you are strongly advised to avoid leaving the U.S. for now, as the details of the order have changed repeatedly and you could be prevented from returning to the U.S. after your trip.</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_and_pre-arrival_documentation">Planning and pre-arrival documentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Planning and pre-arrival documentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Visa-free_entry">Visa-free entry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Visa-free entry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Welcome_to_the_US.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikivoyage/en/thumb/1/1c/Welcome_to_the_US.jpg/250px-Welcome_to_the_US.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikivoyage/en/thumb/1/1c/Welcome_to_the_US.jpg/375px-Welcome_to_the_US.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikivoyage/en/1/1c/Welcome_to_the_US.jpg 2x" data-file-width="424" data-file-height="329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Welcome_to_the_US.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Welcome!</div></div></div>
<p>Citizens of the 38 countries within the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://travel.state.gov/content/travel/en/us-visas/tourism-visit/visa-waiver-program.html">Visa Waiver Program</a></b> (<b>VWP</b>), as well as Canadians and Bermudians do not require visas for entry into the United States.  <b>Canadians</b> and <b>Bermudians</b> are normally allowed to visit for up to <b>six months</b>. Permanent residents of Canada are <i>not</i> eligible for visa-free entry, unless they are also citizens of a country that participates in the Visa Waiver Program, or one of the separate provisions for a few other countries.
</p><p>The Visa Waiver Program permits visa-free stays of up to <b>90 days</b>; it applies to citizens of Andorra, Austria, Australia, Belgium, Brunei, Chile, Czech Republic, Denmark, Estonia, Finland, France, Germany, Greece, Hungary, Iceland, Ireland, Italy, Japan, South Korea, Latvia, Liechtenstein, Lithuania, Luxembourg, Malta, Monaco, the Netherlands, New Zealand, Norway, Portugal, San Marino, Singapore, Slovakia, Slovenia, Spain, Sweden, Switzerland, Taiwan (must include ID card number), and the United Kingdom (must have right of abode in the UK, Channel Islands or Isle of Man).
</p><p>Citizens of the Federated States of Micronesia, the Marshall Islands, and Palau may enter, reside, study, and work in the U.S. indefinitely with only a valid passport.
</p><p>Citizens of the Bahamas may apply for visa-free entry <i>only</i> at the U.S. Customs pre-clearance facilities in the Bahamas, but a valid police certificate that was issued within the last six months is required for those over the age of 14. Attempting to enter through any other port of entry requires a valid visa.
</p><p>Citizens of the Turks and Caicos Islands may enter the U.S. without a visa <i>only</i> if they are travelling on a direct flight from there, but a valid police certificate issued within the last six months is required for those over the age of 14. Attempting to enter from any other country requires a valid visa.
</p><p>Cayman Islands citizens, if they intend to travel directly to the U.S. from there, may obtain a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.immigration.gov.ky/portal/page/portal/immhome/help/forms/usvisaapp">single-entry visa waiver</a> for about $25 prior to departure. A valid police certificate that was issued within the last three months is required for those over the age of 13. Attempting to enter from any other country will require you to have a valid visa.
</p><p>A criminal record will likely revoke any right to visa-free travel to the U.S. Although there are exceptions e.g. traffic violations, civil infractions (such as littering, noise violations, disorderly conduct), purely political offenses (e.g. non-violent protest in countries where it is not allowed), and offenses committed before the age of 16. Anyone with a criminal record, including Canadians and Bermudians, should seek advice from a U.S. embassy on whether they need to obtain a visa.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Visa_Waiver_Program_requirements">Visa Waiver Program requirements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Visa Waiver Program requirements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div style="display: table; background-color: #faf0ff; border: 1px solid #507; margin: 0.5em 1em 0.5em 1em; padding: 6px;" class="pp_warningbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td style="width: 56px;"><img alt="Travel Warning" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/50px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Travel Warning" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/75px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/100px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="765" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size: 130%; color: #507"><b>Visa restrictions:</b></span>
<div style="font-size: 100%">Under new rules passed in 2015, travellers who have visited <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> on or after March 1, 2011, are not eligible to enter under the VWP. They remain eligible to apply for a regular tourism or business visa – at the expense of more cost and hassle than with the Visa Waiver Program (VWP).
Similarly, dual citizens who hold the citizenship of Iran, Iraq, Sudan, or Syria in addition to a nationality otherwise eligible for a visa waiver cannot enter under the VWP.</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>The program is open only to travellers who are in the United States for tourism or business purposes.  You cannot be coming to the U.S. for formal education, to get a job, or to conduct journalism; if you are, you must get an appropriate visa in advance no matter how short your trip to the U.S. may be.
</p><p>The 90-day limit is not extendable. A short trip to Canada, Mexico, or the Caribbean will not allow a fresh 90 days upon return to the U.S. An extended absence to the neighboring countries may reset the limit, particularly if your first trip to the U.S. was short. Take care if transiting through the U.S. on a trip around North America that exceeds 90 days.
</p><p>Having a criminal record, having been refused entry, or having been denied a U.S. visa will make you ineligible to enter on the VWP; you will have to apply for a U.S. visa instead.
</p><p>Entry under the VWP by air or sea requires the completion of an online form and a payment of $14, preferably 72 hours before arrival. The form is called the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://esta.cbp.dhs.gov/">Electronic System for Travel Authorization</a></b> (ESTA). ESTA approval covers multiple trips and is valid for two years (unless your passport expires earlier). This requirement is waived if entering by land.
</p><p><b>All passports</b> must be biometric. If your passport is an older one that was issued before biometric passports were available, you will need to obtain a new passport to travel to the U.S. on the VWP.
</p><p>Entry under the VWP by air or sea requires travel with a signatory carrier. Any commercial scheduled services to the U.S. will be fine, but if you are on a chartered flight or vessel you should check the status of the carrier, as you may require a visa. Flying your own personal aircraft, or sailing your own personal yacht to the U.S. will require you to obtain a tourist visa in advance.
</p><p>Travellers entering by air or sea should also have a return or onward ticket out of the United States. This requirement is not necessary for residents of Canada, Mexico, Bermuda, or the Caribbean.
</p><p>Entry under the VWP does not allow you to change your immigration status, and if you are denied entry, the decision can't be appealed and you will immediately be placed on the first flight out.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Obtaining_a_visa">Obtaining a visa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Obtaining a visa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="pp_infobox" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left: 1em; width:40%;" cellpadding="8" cellspacing="1">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="background-color:#effafa; font-size:90%; border:1px solid #ccc; padding-left: 1em; padding-right: 1em; text-align:left; width:100%;">
<p><b>U.S. Visa/Residence Status Overview</b>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<ul><li><b>B-1</b>: Business visitor</li>
<li><b>B-2</b>: Tourist ("visitor for pleasure")</li>
<li><b>B-1/B-2</b>: Combo visa that can be used for either or both tourism and business</li>
<li><b>C-1</b>: Transit</li>
<li><b>F-1</b>: Academic Student</li>
<li><b>H-1B</b> / <b>L-1</b>: Employment</li>
<li><b>J-1</b>: Exchange Program / Postdoctoral Researcher</li>
<li><b>M-1</b>: Vocational Student</li>
<li><b>O-1</b> / <b>P-1</b>: Sportsperson / Performing Artiste</li>
<li><b>WB</b>: Visa Waiver Program, Business; not extendable past 90 days</li>
<li><b>WT</b>: Visa Waiver Program, Tourist; not extendable past 90 days</li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>For the rest of the world, the visa application fee is a non-refundable <b>$160</b> (as of October 2018) for visas that are not issued on the basis of a petition and $190 for those that are; this fee is waived under very limited circumstances, namely for people requesting certain exchange visitor visas.
</p><p>Depending on your nationality and the category of visa you are requesting, you may need to pay an additional fee (ranging from $7–200) only if the visa is issued. This is called a <i>reciprocity fee</i> and is charged by the U.S. to match the fees charged by other countries on U.S. citizens.
</p><p>Additionally, Chinese (PRC) citizens (that is, anyone travelling to the US on a PRC passport) <b>must enroll</b> in the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.evus.gov/evus/#/">Electronic Visa Update System</a></b> (EVUS) for travel into the United States on any 10-year B-type visa. The enrollment is valid for two years (or until passport/visa expiry, whichever comes first) before it needs to be updated again.
</p><p>The Immigration and Nationality Act states that all persons requesting entry into the U.S. as non-immigrants are presumed to be immigrants until they overcome that presumption by showing evidence of "binding ties" to their home country, and sufficient proof that the visit will be temporary. Applicants also need to demonstrate that they are genuinely entitled to the visa they are applying for. Face-to-face interviews at the nearest U.S. embassy or consulate are required for almost all nationalities, and waits for interview slots and visa processing can add up to several months.
</p><p>Embassies are closed on U.S. holidays <i>and</i> on holidays of the host country, so you need to know both holidays when setting dates to apply for a visa. You should start planning your trip far in advance, as the application process is known to take up to six months.
</p><p><i>Do not assume anything.</i> Check on documentation requirements with the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://travel.state.gov/">U.S. State Department</a> or with the nearest U.S. consulate.
</p><p>Your visa is generally not tied to your permitted length of stay; for example, a 10-year visa does not allow a stay of 10 years. On the other hand, you can enter the country on the last day of validity of your visa and still be allowed to stay, for example, up to 180 days as a tourist.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Statueofliberty_nps.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Statueofliberty_nps.jpg/250px-Statueofliberty_nps.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="375" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Statueofliberty_nps.jpg/375px-Statueofliberty_nps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Statueofliberty_nps.jpg/500px-Statueofliberty_nps.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Statueofliberty_nps.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Liberty, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel_to_U.S._possessions">Travel to U.S. possessions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Travel to U.S. possessions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>America's overseas possessions have slightly different rules. See each destination's article for details.
</p><p>In brief, <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Virgin_Islands" title="U.S. Virgin Islands">U.S. Virgin Islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a> all have the same entry requirements as the 50 states. However, Guam and the Northern Marianas apply the visa waiver program to a few additional countries, while the U.S. Virgin Islands also allows visa-free entry to citizens of the British Virgin Islands.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a> lies outside the federal immigration jurisdiction and has separate entry requirements.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arriving_in_the_United_States">Arriving in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Arriving in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_Entry" title="Global Entry">Global Entry</a>, <a href="/wiki/NEXUS" title="NEXUS">NEXUS</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>On arrival, you will be questioned briefly at immigration to determine if your purpose of entry matches your visa class. If you are unable to convince immigration officers that you intend to abide by the terms of your visa, you will be refused entry and deported.
</p><p>Once they decide to let you in, you are <b>fingerprinted</b> and a <b>digital photograph</b> is taken. Entry will be denied if either of these procedures is refused.
</p><p>At selected airports, Canadian and VWP nationals may be able to use automated passport control (APC) kiosks to record their passport and biometric details. Household members traveling together can do this at once. VWP nationals need to have ESTA clearance, and have entered the U.S. at least once since 2008. If successful, the traveller gets a receipt and goes to the designated CBP desk to continue the inspection process. Canadians and other selected nationals may be eligible to participate in various trusted traveler programs such as <a href="/wiki/Global_Entry" title="Global Entry">Global Entry</a> and <a href="/wiki/NEXUS" title="NEXUS">NEXUS</a>, which allow pre-approved passengers to use a designated kiosk for the inspection process. Unlike APC, these programs require prior application, background checks, an interview, and an application fee, but allows the passenger to bypass intense questioning and skip the lines at immigration for up to 5 years.
</p><p>Because <b>marijuana</b> is illegal under federal law, if you have consumed marijuana or invested in the marijuana industry, you can be banned from entering the United States. This is true even if marijuana is legal in your country and even if it's also legal in the state you're trying to enter. Border officials don't usually ask about marijuana, but if they see a reason to ask, they might. Even a single copy of "High Times" magazine or anything similar is enough to draw suspicion. If you admit to having used marijuana (or any other drug illegal under U.S. federal law) or invested in the industry, or if you are caught lying about it, you can be turned back and <b>banned for life</b> from entering the country. To appeal the ban, you have to apply for a $585 waiver.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs">Customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Each household (i.e. family members living and traveling together) needs to complete one customs declaration form. Travellers eligible for APC, as well as Global Entry and NEXUS members, can do this electronically using the respective kiosks and need not fill up the paper form. Those eligible for <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://mobilepassport.us/">Mobile Passport Control</a> (U.S. citizens and some Canadian citizens) also do not need to fill out the form, provided they have completed the app's questions and have a QR code ready to go. Detailed and up-to-date information on prohibited and restricted items can be found at the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.cbp.gov/travel/us-citizens/know-before-you-go/prohibited-and-restricted-items">relevant government website</a>.
</p><p>Do not attempt to import items originating from countries against which the U.S. has imposed economic sanctions (<a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>); they will be confiscated by customs if discovered. An exception to this rule is cigars and alcoholic beverages (mainly rum) originating from Cuba. With the ongoing thaw in <a href="/wiki/Americans_in_Cuba" title="Americans in Cuba">U.S.–Cuban relations</a>, the U.S. has lifted sanctions against importation of these items for personal use (limits are the same as for tobacco and alcohol originating elsewhere).
</p><p>The United States has very strict biosecurity laws, and imposes restrictions on the types of food that may be brought into the country. In general, fresh food may not be brought into the country, though some types of processed, commercially packaged food may be allowed, depending on the country of origin. Check with the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.aphis.usda.gov/aphis/ourfocus/planthealth/sa_international/sa_travel?1dmy&amp;urile=wcm%3apath%3a%2Faphis_content_library%2Fsa_resources%2Fsa_traveler%2Fct_general_allowed_food_items">United States Department of Agriculture (USDA)</a></b> for more details. <b>All</b> food and plant items being brought into the country must declared and inspected by customs, even if permitted. Failure to declare agricultural products can result in a fine, or in serious cases even prosecution.
</p><p>Besides your personal effects, which will go home with you, you are allowed to import individual gifts with a value of $100 or less per item. If you're 21 years of age or older, you may also import limited quantities of <b>tobacco</b> and <b>alcohol</b> products duty-free:
</p>
<ul><li>Up to 200 cigarettes (one carton), or up to 50 cigars, or up to 2&#160;kg of loose tobacco products such as snuff, or a proportional combination thereof.</li>
<li>Up to 1 liter of alcohol. Unlike some countries, the one-liter restriction applies irrespective of strength: a fifth of Scotch at 40% ABV or a standard 750&#160;mL bottle of wine at 14% ABV are both within the allowance, but a six-pack of 12-ounce beers at 5% ABV is almost 2 liters and is over the duty-free allowance.</li></ul>
<p>If you are over the alcohol exemption by a small amount (e.g. a six pack of beer or a second bottle of wine) most customs officers will let this slide for wine and beer <i>if you've made a full and accurate declaration</i>. Anything more than this, or any hard spirits over the limit will likely result in duty and tax being assessed, the amount of which depends in part on the state you're entering to and the country the goods are from. (Duty from Canada, for example, is minimal owing in part to NAFTA.) <b>Customs officers do not show this leniency with tobacco products</b>; expect to pay if you are even one cigarette over!
</p><p>A reasonable quantity of <b>perfume</b> or <b>cologne</b> can also be imported provided the brand is not under a "Trademark Restriction in the United States". There is no restriction on the amount of <b>money</b> you can bring into or out of the U.S. However, if you are travelling with $10,000 or more (or its equivalent in foreign currency) per household, you must declare it on your customs form and you will be given a special form to fill out; not declaring exposes you to a fine and possible seizure of that cash.  Checks, bonds and other financial instruments must also be declared.  ATM/Debit cards linked to non-U.S. bank accounts carrying the said amount do not need to be declared (although your bank may impose some withdrawal restrictions and fees to access this money in the U.S.).
</p><p>The U.S. possessions of American Samoa, Guam, the Northern Marianas Islands, and U.S. Virgin Islands are outside the federal customs jurisdiction and each have their own separate requirements. Travel between these regions and the rest of the U.S. requires a customs check. There are some differences (mostly larger) in duty exemptions for U.S. citizens returning from these destinations.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaving_the_United_States">Leaving the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Leaving the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div style="display:table; align:center; background-color:#ffffdf; border:1px solid #888822; margin:0.5em 1em 0 1em; padding:6px;" class="pp_cautionbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><img alt="Caution" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/40px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Caution" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/60px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/80px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size:130%; color:#000000;"><b>Thinking of Overstaying?:</b></span> Overstaying the period granted at passport control or violating your terms of entry (e.g. work on a B1/B2 status) <b>will automatically invalidate your visa.</b> It will also make it extremely difficult to re-enter the United States and may also bar you from re-entry for at least three years, if not permanently. If you overstayed on the Visa Waiver Program, you will <b>need a visa</b> for <b>all</b> future visits.
<p>If you overstay for compelling reasons such as medical emergencies and flight delays or cancellations, you will need to keep immigration officials informed of your situation in order to avoid any of the above sanctions.
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>Unlike most countries, the U.S. has no formal passport control upon exit, especially for those travelling by air or sea.  As such, your airline or shipping company will document your departure and report it to the U.S. Customs and Border Protection (CBP).  The CBP then updates your immigration record.  Aliens who arrived in the U.S. by air or sea after mid-2013, and depart by air or sea do not need to do anything further.
</p><p>If you fall into one of the following categories, you may need to take further action to actively prove that you left the U.S. on time:
</p>
<ol><li>last arrived in the U.S. before mid-2013 through any means (when the paper I-94 card was still physically issued to foreigners): turn over the I-94 card to the airline staff at check-in, or to the Canadian or Mexican immigration officer if departing by land</li>
<li>arrived in the U.S. via land or private vehicles (paper I-94 cards are still issued here): turnover the I-94 card to the airline staff at check-in, or to the Canadian or Mexican immigration officer if departing by land</li>
<li>left the U.S. via land or private vehicles: save any evidence that you were outside the U.S. before your authorised stay expired</li></ol>
<p>In any case, on future visits, consider bringing the necessary documents to prove you left legally.  U.S. Customs and Border Protection has <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://help.cbp.gov/s/article/Article-752?language=en_US">information</a> about what to do if your slip is not collected.
</p><p>If you take a side trip to Canada or Mexico by land and return within 30 days or the allowed time of your stay (whichever is shorter), you may re-enter the U.S. provided that you do <b>not yet</b> return any issued I-94 card before you proceed to Canada or Mexico.  This can also be done even if you entered the U.S. on a single-entry visa, or the visa you entered the U.S. with has expired.  However, you will only be admitted for the remainder of your original allowed time; the deadline to leave the U.S. won't be extended by just leaving for somewhere else in North America.  If you return the I-94 while on the side trip, you will have to apply all over again to enter the U.S. (which means a new visa for single-entry visa holders) and be subject to the usual questioning.
</p><p>Try to avoid re-entering the U.S. a few days, weeks or months after one visit.  Even if you don't overstay, planning several U.S. visits spaced shortly after each other may be interpreted by immigration officers as "immigrant intent" and cause you grief.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_plane">By plane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: By plane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div style="display:table; align:center; background-color:#ffffdf; border:1px solid #888822; margin:0.5em 1em 0 1em; padding:6px;" class="pp_cautionbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><img alt="Caution" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/40px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Caution" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/60px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/80px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size:130%; color:#000000;"><b>Enhanced Security Measures:</b></span> Under new rules implemented on October 26, 2017, passengers on flights to the United States are subject to more rigorous checks and security measures prior to boarding. Depending on the airline and point of departure, this may include a comprehensive bag search, as well as rigorous questioning by security personnel for all passengers prior to being allowed to board the aircraft. Be sure to arrive at the check-in counter at least 3 hours before your scheduled departure time, and also arrive at the boarding gate early so you have enough time to complete all security procedures.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg/250px-Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg/375px-Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Needle_Mount_Ranier_Seattle_Washington_USA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> skyline</div></div></div>
<p>Most visitors from outside Canada and Mexico arrive in the United States by plane. While many medium sized inland cities have an international airport, there are limited flights to most of these and most travelers find themselves entering the U.S. at one of the major entry points along the coasts.  The international airports in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Hartsfield%E2%80%93Jackson_Atlanta_International_Airport#By_plane" title="Hartsfield–Jackson Atlanta International Airport">ATL</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>), <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Newark_Liberty_International_Airport#By_plane" title="Newark Liberty International Airport">EWR</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup> &amp; <b><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport#By_plane" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">JFK</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>; for <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area_airport_codes" title="Metropolitan area airport codes">all airports</a>, <b><a href="/wiki/New_York_City#By_plane" title="New York City">NYC</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport#By_plane" title="Los Angeles International Airport">LAX</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/O%27Hare_International_Airport#By_plane" title="O&#39;Hare International Airport">ORD</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>; for all airports, <b><a href="/wiki/Chicago#By_plane" title="Chicago">CHI</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>), <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_International_Airport#By_plane" title="San Francisco International Airport">SFO</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Seattle-Tacoma_International_Airport#By_plane" title="Seattle-Tacoma International Airport">SEA</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/Miami_International_Airport#By_plane" title="Miami International Airport">MIA</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a> (<b><a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Intercontinental_Airport#By_plane" title="George Bush Intercontinental Airport">IAH</a></b><sup><small><a href="/wiki/IATA" class="mw-redirect" title="IATA">&#160;IATA</a></small></sup>) are the main points of entry to the United States by plane.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg/250px-Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg/375px-Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg/500px-Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diamond_Head_from_Waikiki_Beach.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diamond Head &amp; Waikiki Beach, <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a></div></div></div>
<p>In general, major cities on the east coast have the best connections to Europe, Africa, the Caribbean and the Middle East, while major cities on the west coast have the best connections to East Asia, Southeast Asia and Oceania. Most sizeable cities would be served by at least one flight to a major Canadian city, while New York City, Miami, Los Angeles and Houston generally provide the best connections to Latin America. Direct air travel to/from <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> was restored from Miami after a 55-year embargo was relaxed in 2014, but some Office of Foreign Asset Control (OFAC) restrictions continue to apply; see the <a href="/wiki/Americans_in_Cuba" title="Americans in Cuba">Americans in Cuba</a> article.
</p><p>Unlike most other countries, the U.S. has never had a flag carrier. The "big three" legacy carriers, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.united.com">United Airlines</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.aa.com">American Airlines</a></b> and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.delta.com">Delta Air Lines</a></b>, are among the largest airlines in the world, and operate flights from various cities around the globe into their respective hubs. Other smaller U.S.-based airlines also fly internationally, though options are usually limited to destinations within the Americas. Most major European and East Asian airlines also fly from their respective countries into several of the major hubs, with <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.britishairways.com">British Airways</a></b> in particular having one of the most comprehensive networks into U.S. cities from their hub in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">Heathrow</a>.
</p><p>The U.S. requires full entry formalities even for international transit. If you normally need a visa to visit the U.S. and can't <a href="/wiki/Avoiding_travel_through_the_United_States" title="Avoiding travel through the United States">avoid a transit</a>, you will need at least a C-1 transit visa. If you are transferring to a domestic flight, you will need to go through customs and immigration at your first U.S. stop; make sure you allow ample time to make any transfers.
</p><p>Most airports have near the exit a wall of "courtesy phones" with the description and the prices of motels in the area. You can call these motels free of charge and ask for a room and a pick up shuttle will come to fetch you at the airport. It is very convenient and mostly free of charge (but you are supposed to tip the driver).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Airport_security">Airport security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Airport security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg/220px-Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="366" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg/330px-Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg/440px-Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="1899" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Travel_Sentry_Lock.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>TSA approved lock with symbol</div></div></div>
<p>Security at U.S. airports is onerous, especially during busy holiday periods, and security procedures for commercial flights from the U.S. continue to evolve. Allow plenty of time (at least 15 minutes, sometimes more than 1 hour) and pack as lightly as possible. Security is handled by the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.tsa.gov/">Transportation Security Administration</a></b> (<b>TSA</b>), although details can vary slightly at each airport. Adults must show <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tsa.gov/travel/security-screening/identification">approved picture ID</a></b> (a passport is sufficient).
</p><p>When connecting from an international flight, <i>all</i> passengers must go through security screening to continue on the onward flight, after clearing immigration and customs inspections. That means all liquids and prohibited items (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tsa.gov/traveler-information/prohibited-items">per TSA rules</a>) that were purchased in a Duty Free shop or allowed through as carry on from a foreign airport must re-packed into checked luggage after coming out of the customs area and before re-checking luggage. In most airports there is a check-in desk outside or conveyor belt outside of customs for transiting passengers to re-check their luggage. Items cannot be re-packed or re-arranged before customs inspections in the luggage reclaim area.
</p><p>There are limitations on <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tsa.gov/travel/security-screening/liquids-rule">liquids</a></b> (including gels, aerosols, creams, and pastes) in carry-on baggage. Ensure that any liquids are held in containers no bigger than 3.4 ounces (100&#160;mL). The containers must all be placed within a single zippered plastic bag that is 1 quart (946&#160;mL) or less in size. Only one such bag, with however much liquid, is allowed per passenger. Medications (including saline solution for contact lenses) and infant and child nourishment are exempt but subject to additional testing; notify TSA agents if you are carrying these items, store them separately from your other liquids, and if possible clearly label them in advance.
</p><p>If you wish to lock your checked baggage, the TSA requires you to use special locks that have the Travel Sentry TSA lock system. These locks can be opened by TSA officials using a master key should they wish to inspect the contents of your bag. If your lock is not one of the TSA-approved locks, the TSA will break it open and you will not be entitled to any compensation for the damage.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-clearance">Pre-clearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Pre-clearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dt>From Canada</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<p>Passengers whose journeys begin in major Canadian airports and involve either U.S. or Canadian carriers will <i>usually</i> be able to clear U.S. entry formalities (passport control and customs) at their Canadian port of exit. These flights are treated the same as U.S. domestic flights, and most Canadian carriers are located in U.S. domestic terminals or concourses in most airports. Some airports (such as LaGuardia Airport in New York City) that don't have customs and immigration facilities receive pre-cleared flights from Canada.
</p><p>Travellers on U.S.–Canadian flights operated by foreign carriers like Philippine Airlines and Cathay Pacific, and those from minor Canadian airports that do not have preclearance facilities, will still encounter traditional entry formalities upon arrival at their first U.S. stop; a Canadian transit visa may be required even if passengers are confined to a holding area for the entire transit time.
</p><p>Some airports in Canada, including <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a> International Airport, Terminal 1 of <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>-Pearson Airport, and <a href="/wiki/Montr%C3%A9al" class="mw-redirect" title="Montréal">Montréal</a>-Trudeau Airport generally do not require passengers in transit from abroad to pass through Canadian Customs and Immigration controls before going through U.S. preclearance formalities. However, even if you pass through these airports, make sure that your papers are in order to allow you to enter Canada: if you cannot travel to the U.S. on the same day you go through preclearance, if you are not cleared for entry to the United States, <i>or</i> if you and/or your luggage is not checked through by your airline to at least your first destination in the United States, you must report to Canada Customs; a Canadian transit or temporary resident visa may be required. This arrangement does not apply in the reverse direction, meaning that you must pass through Canadian customs and immigration on your flight out.
</p>
<dl><dt>From other countries</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<p>Preclearance facilities are available at Queen Beatrix International Airport in <a href="/wiki/Aruba" title="Aruba">Aruba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Bahama" title="Grand Bahama">Grand Bahama</a> and Lynden Pindling International Airports in the <a href="/wiki/Bahamas" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> International Airport, <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shannon" title="Shannon">Shannon</a> International Airports in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a> International Airport in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>.
</p><p>Passengers on British Airways flights from <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> to New York City transiting via either Dublin or Shannon, Ireland can take advantage of U.S. passport control and customs preclearance at Dublin or Shannon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_car">By car</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: By car">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div style="display: table; background-color: #faf0ff; border: 1px solid #507; margin: 0.5em 1em 0.5em 1em; padding: 6px;" class="pp_warningbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td style="width: 56px;"><img alt="Travel Warning" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/50px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Travel Warning" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/75px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aiga_immigration_inv.svg/100px-Aiga_immigration_inv.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="765" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size: 130%; color: #507"><b>Visa restrictions:</b></span>
<div style="font-size: 100%">All persons wishing to enter the United States by land <b>must possess a valid passport</b>; <a href="/wiki/NEXUS" title="NEXUS">NEXUS</a>, FAST, <a href="/wiki/Global_Entry" title="Global Entry">Global Entry</a>, SENTRI, or passport card; Laser Visa; or an "enhanced driver's license" (issued by certain U.S. states and Canadian provinces)</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>The U.S.–Canada and U.S.–Mexico borders are two of the most frequently crossed borders with millions of crossings daily. Average wait times are up to 30 minutes, but some of the busiest crossings encounter considerable delays—approaching 1–2 hours at peak times (weekends, holidays). Current wait times (updated hourly) are available on the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://apps.cbp.gov/bwt/">U.S. customs service website</a>. The U.S.–Mexico border is lucrative for drug trafficking, so vehicles crossing may be X-rayed or searched by a drug-sniffing dog. If there is suspicion, your vehicle may be searched. Since this is an all-too-common event, expect no patience from border agents.
</p><p>Foreigners entering by land are required to pay a $6 fee when crossing the border. No fee is payable if you made a side trip to Canada or Mexico and are simply re-entering the U.S.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_bus">By bus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: By bus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greyhound offers inexpensive cross-border service from Canada and Mexico. Some routes, such as Toronto to Buffalo, have hourly service. Megabus U.S. also runs daily trips from Toronto (also a hub for Megabus Canada) to New York City via <a href="/wiki/Buffalo" title="Buffalo">Buffalo</a> for as low as $1.
</p><p>One of the routes of the city bus system of <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor, Ontario</a> (the Tunnel Bus) takes passengers to <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> -- pedestrians or bicycles are not allowed on the bridge, in the tunnel, or on the ferry.
</p><p>Bus passengers often experience greater scrutiny from U.S. customs officials than car or train passengers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellis_Island-27527.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Ellis_Island-27527.jpg/220px-Ellis_Island-27527.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Ellis_Island-27527.jpg/330px-Ellis_Island-27527.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Ellis_Island-27527.jpg/440px-Ellis_Island-27527.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellis_Island-27527.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ellis_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellis Island">Ellis Island</a>, the main historic terminus for immigrants coming from Europe, Africa, the Middle East, and parts of South America.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_boat">By boat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: By boat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Entry by sea is not very common today. The most common entry points for private boats are <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> and other Eastern coastal states. Some passenger ferries from Canada exist, mostly between <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington State">Washington State</a> or Alaska.
</p><p><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cunard.com/">Cunard</a></b> offers transatlantic ship travel between the United Kingdom and New York City.
</p><p>The ferries between <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> state are treated as land border crossings rather than sea entry points. This means that while ESTA is not required for VWP visitors, visitors entering the U.S. this way will be required to pay the $6 entry fee levied at land border crossings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_train">By train</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: By train">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Amtrak" class="mw-redirect" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> offers international service from the Canadian cities of Vancouver (<b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amtrakcascades.com">Amtrak Cascades</a></b> has two trips per day to Seattle), <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> (<b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amtrak.com/maple-leaf-train">Maple Leaf</a></b> once daily to New York City via <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> (<b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amtrak.com/adirondack-train">Adirondack</a></b> once daily to New York City via Albany).
</p><p>On international trains from Montreal and Toronto, immigration formalities are conducted at the border; this takes significantly more time than it would on a bus, which means the bus is often less expensive and faster than the train.
</p><p>Travellers from Vancouver clear U.S. immigration and customs at Pacific Central Station before they get on the train itself, just as they do for air travel. <b>Be sure to allow enough time for inspections.</b>
</p><p>From Mexico the nearest Amtrak stations are in <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yuma" title="Yuma">Yuma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Del_Rio" title="Del Rio">Del Rio</a> and <a href="/wiki/El_Paso" title="El Paso">El Paso</a>. Amtrak trains do not cross the border into Mexico so passengers continue to the border by local public transportation or by taxi from the Amtrak station. There are no passenger trains to the border from anywhere in Mexico.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_foot">By foot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: By foot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many border crossings in urban areas which can be crossed by pedestrians. Crossings such as those in or near <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls_(New_York)" title="Niagara Falls (New York)">Niagara Falls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tijuana" title="Tijuana">Tijuana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nogales_(Arizona)" title="Nogales (Arizona)">Nogales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/El_Paso" title="El Paso">El Paso</a> are popular for persons wishing to spend a day on the other side of the border. In some cases, this may be ideal for day-trippers, as crossing by car can be a much longer wait.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Get_around">Get around</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Get around">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
