<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg/250px-Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg/375px-Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg/500px-Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Us-ca-sanfran-goldengate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Golden Gate Bridge in San Francisco, California</div></div></div>
<p>The size of the U.S. and the distance separating major cities make air the dominant mode of travel for short-term travellers. If you have time, travel by car, bus, or <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" class="mw-redirect" title="Amtrak">rail</a> can be interesting.
</p><p>Some states offer traffic and public transport information by dialing 511 on your phone.
</p><p><b>Quarantine restrictions</b> are in place when traveling between the continental U.S. and <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_plane_2">By plane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: By plane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Flying_in_the_United_States" title="Flying in the United States">Flying in the United States</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>The quickest and often the most convenient method of long-distance intercity travel in the U.S. is by plane. Coast-to-coast travel takes about 6 hours from east to west, and 5 hours from west to east (varying due to winds), compared to the days necessary for land transportation. Most large cities in the U.S. are served by one or two airports; many smaller towns also have some passenger air service, although you may need to detour through a major hub airport to get there. Depending on where you are starting, it may be cheaper to travel by bus, train, or car to a nearby large city and fly or, conversely, to fly to a large city near your destination and travel by ground to your destination.
</p><p>The largest airlines are the three remaining mainline legacy carriers (<b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.aa.com">American Airlines</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.delta.com">Delta</a></b>, and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.united.com">United</a></b>) and two of the country's low-cost carriers, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.southwest.com">Southwest</a></b> and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jetblue.com">JetBlue</a></b>. <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.alaskaair.com">Alaska Airlines</a></b> and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.hawaiianairlines.com">Hawaiian Airlines</a></b> are legacy regional carriers, while smaller airlines <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.spirit.com">Spirit</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.flyfrontier.com">Frontier</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.allegiant.com">Allegiant</a></b>  and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.suncountry.com">Sun Country</a></b> are trying to make inroads. There are also smaller regional airlines that are subsidiaries of the mainline carriers and can be booked through their parents.
</p><p>Major carriers compete for business on major routes, and travellers willing to book two or more weeks in advance can get bargains. However most smaller destinations are served by only one or two regional carriers, and prices there can be expensive. The difference in fees and service between low-cost and mainline carriers is, however, virtually nonexistent these days. Low-cost carriers occasionally offer more amenities than mainline carriers, such as inflight entertainment for even a short-haul flight, or free checked baggage in the price of their tickets. Southwest Airlines, for instance, allows passengers to check in up to two pieces of bags in their base price.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Security">Security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dd><i>See <a href="#Airport_security">&#167; Security</a> under <a href="#By_plane">&#167; Get in - By plane</a></i></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_private_plane">By private plane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: By private plane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/General_aviation" title="General aviation">General aviation</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>The cost of chartering the smallest private jet begins at around $4000 per flight hour, with the cost substantially higher for larger, longer-range aircraft, and cheaper for smaller propeller planes.  While private flying is by no means inexpensive, a family of four or more can often fly together at a cost similar to or even favorable to buying first-class commercial airline tickets, especially to smaller airports where scheduled commercial flights are at their most expensive, and private flying is at its cheapest. Though you may find it cheaper than flying a family of four first-class internationally, it is rarely the case, except when traveling from Western Europe. General aviation is the most practical way to reach the outer boroughs of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>.
</p><p><b>Air charter</b> refers to hiring a private plane for a one-time journey.  <b>Jet Cards</b> are pre-paid cards entitling the owner to a specific number of flight hours on a specified aircraft.  As all expenses are pre-paid on the card, you need not concern yourself with deadhead time, return flights, landing fees, etc.
</p><p>Many small-town airports on America's borders welcome individually-owned small aircraft. Give them an hour or two advance notice so that they can fetch border officials to meet the tiny private plane from exotic and foreign <a href="/wiki/Brockville" title="Brockville">Brockville</a>, and you've provided just the excuse they needed to add "International Airport" to their names.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_train_2">By train</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: By train">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Rail_travel_in_the_United_States" title="Rail travel in the United States">Rail travel in the United States</a></i></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg/220px-Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg/330px-Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg/440px-Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2053" data-file-height="1739" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westbound_Southwest_Chief_-_Colorado.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Westbound Southwest Chief in Colorado</div></div></div>
<p>Due to the popularity of flying and cars, the passenger rail network in the United States is a shadow of what it was a century ago. While the United States still has the world's longest rail network, it is primarily used for freight transport these days. Except for certain corridors (mostly in the Northeast where a second cousin of <a href="/wiki/High_speed_rail" class="mw-redirect" title="High speed rail">high speed rail</a> is available), passenger trains in the United States can be surprisingly scarce, slow, unreliable, and expensive. The national rail system, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amtrak.com">Amtrak</a></b> <abbr title="phone" style="text-decoration:none">☏</abbr> <bdi class="tel listing-phone"><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="tel:+1-800-872-7245">+1-800-USA-RAIL (872-7245)</a></span></bdi>, provides service to many cities, offering exceptional sightseeing opportunities, but not particularly efficient inter-city travel, and is often more expensive than a flight. In more urban locations, Amtrak can be very efficient and comfortable, but in rural areas delays are common. Plan ahead to ensure train travel between your destinations is available and/or convenient. They have promotional discounts of 15% for students and seniors, and a 30-day U.S. Rail Pass for international travellers only. If you plan to buy a regular ticket within a week of travelling, it pays to check the website for sometimes significant "weekly specials". There is no dedicated <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> network in the United States, and driving yourself will often be quicker than taking the train when travelling long distances.
</p><p>Amtrak offers many amenities and services that are lacking from other modes of transport.  Amtrak's routes traverse some of America's most beautiful areas.  Travellers with limited time may not find travel by train to be convenient, simply because the country is <i>big</i>, and that "bigness" is particularly evident in many of the scenic areas.  For those with ample time, though, train travel offers an unparalleled view of the U.S.. Some of the most scenic routes include the <i><a href="/wiki/California_Zephyr" title="California Zephyr">California Zephyr</a></i> that runs between <a href="/wiki/Emeryville" title="Emeryville">Emeryville</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_Area_(California)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bay Area (California)">San Francisco Bay Area</a> of California to Chicago and the <i>Empire Builder</i> that goes from Chicago to Seattle or Portland. Both offer a lounge car with floor-to-ceiling windows and double decker cars.
</p><p>During usual American vacation times, some long-distance trains (outside the Northeast) can sell out weeks or even months in advance. Booking early also results in generally lower fares for all trains.  Same-day reservations are usually easy, and depending on the rules of the fare you purchased, you can change travel plans on the day itself without fees.
</p><p>Separate from Amtrak, many major cities offer very reliable <b>commuter trains</b> that carry passengers to and from the suburbs or other relatively close-by areas.  Some commuter train stations have park and ride facilities where you can park your car to use the commuter train to get to a city's downtown core where traffic and parking problems complicate car use.  Parking rates at the stations vary (some facilities may be operated by third parties).  Some commuter train systems and services do not operate on weekends and holidays, and even those that do often have greatly reduced frequencies, so it's best to check the system's website to plan ahead. Buy tickets <b>before</b> you board the train as you will either face a substantially more expensive fare or a hefty fine.
</p><p>Several large cities also operate <b>subway or light rail</b> service for local travel within the city or metro area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_boat_2">By boat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: By boat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>America has the largest system of <b>inland waterways</b> of any country in the world. It is entirely possible to navigate around within the United States by boat.  Your choices of watercraft range from self-propelled canoes and kayaks to elaborate houseboats and riverboat cruises.
</p><p>Rivers and canals were key to developing the country, and traversing by boat gives you a unique perspective on the nation and some one of a kind scenery.  Some examples of waterways open to recreational boating and/or scheduled cruises are:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.canals.ny.gov/exvac/index.html">The New York State Canal System</a></b> operates four canals (including the famous <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a>) comprising 524&#160;mi (843&#160;km) of waterway open for recreational and commercial use. See <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)#Get_around#By_boat" title="New York (state)">New York state</a> for details.</li>
<li><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.greatlakes-seaway.com/en/recreational/index.html">The St. Lawrence Seaway</a></b> is now the primary port of entry for large ships into North America. Recreational boaters are welcome, however, the Seaway is designed for very large craft and a minimum boat length of 6&#160;m (20&#160;ft) applies.  The Seaway starts in eastern Canada and goes to the Great Lakes.</li>
<li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a></b> affords north-south access through the interior of the U.S. to the Gulf of Mexico and connects with all major interior waterways, including the <b>Missouri</b> and <b>Ohio Rivers</b>.</li></ul>
<p>Each year, many beginning boaters successfully navigate these waterways.  Any kind of boating requires some preparation and planning. In general, the Coast Guard, Canal and Seaway authorities go out of their way to help recreational boaters. They will also at times give instructions which you are expected to obey immediately. For example, small craft may be asked to give way to larger craft on canals, and weather conditions may require you to stop or change your route.
</p><p>Regular <a href="/wiki/Ferries_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferries in the United States">ferries</a> exist to a variety of destinations along the coasts. In the northwest of the country, you can travel with the ferries of the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Marine_Highway_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Alaska Marine Highway System">Alaska Marine Highway System</a> from <a href="/wiki/Bellingham_(Washington)" title="Bellingham (Washington)">Bellingham (Washington)</a> all the way along Alaska's southern coast to <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Harbor-Unalaska" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch Harbor-Unalaska">Dutch Harbor-Unalaska</a>. As a bonus you get to enjoy beautiful mountain and archipelago scenery. Moreover, much of off-the-beaten-path-Alaska is just accessible by boat. There is no commercial passenger service between the continental U.S. and <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_car_2">By car</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: By car">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Driving_in_the_United_States" title="Driving in the United States">Driving in the United States</a></i></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:High_Five.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/High_Five.jpg/180px-High_Five.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/High_Five.jpg/270px-High_Five.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/High_Five.jpg/360px-High_Five.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:High_Five.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "High Five" five-Level interchange in <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a></div></div></div>
<p>America's love affair with the automobile is legendary, so travelling the <a href="/wiki/United_States_without_a_car" title="United States without a car">United States without a car</a> can be difficult. Most American cities have developed with automobiles in mind, so renting or bringing your own car is usually a very good idea.  There are only a few major cities where using public transportation is preferable to driving: New York City, Chicago, Boston, San Francisco, and Washington. Other very large cities like Los Angeles, Atlanta, and Miami have limited public transport options, and the options only get worse in smaller cities. Taxis and ride-hailing services are often available, but they can get expensive and taxis (especially) can be hard to find outside of airports. While most Americans are happy to give driving directions, don't be surprised if many aren't familiar with the local public transport options available.
</p><p>Traffic signs do not conform to international standards but, if you understand English, they should be self-explanatory.
</p><p>The national road system consists of 
</p>
<ul><li>Interstate highways, which are controlled-access divided highways with no grade crossings,</li>
<li>the older U.S. Numbered Highway System on surface streets which can go as small as one lane for each direction, and</li>
<li>state routes.</li></ul>
<p>Be careful with road signs, as any of these can morph into state routes, interstates, or highways with little warning other than size and signs. 
</p><p>All of them are generally well maintained by the respective states, but while the main Interstate system generally links only the major towns of every state (hence the name: <i>Interstate</i>), the U.S. highway and state routes can lead you to many interesting off-the-beaten-path sights, if you don't mind stopping at traffic lights and dealing with pedestrians. Most sections of the roads are free to use, but there are some which levy fees.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_American_Road_Trip">Great American Road Trip</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Great American Road Trip">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amboy_(California,_USA),_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Amboy_%28California%2C_USA%29%2C_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg/220px-Amboy_%28California%2C_USA%29%2C_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Amboy_%28California%2C_USA%29%2C_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg/330px-Amboy_%28California%2C_USA%29%2C_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Amboy_%28California%2C_USA%29%2C_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg/440px-Amboy_%28California%2C_USA%29%2C_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5065" data-file-height="2570" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amboy_(California,_USA),_Hist._Route_66_--_2012_--_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Route_66" title="Route 66">Route 66</a>, a highway connecting Los Angeles with Chicago. Essentially the main route for the Great American Road trip. It also invokes nostalgia in the southwestern states.</div></div></div>
<p>A romantic appeal is attached to the idea of <b>long-distance car travel</b>; many Americans will tell you that you can't see the "real" America except by car. Given the scarcity of public transportation in most American cities, the loss of time travelling <i>between</i> cities by car rather than flying can be made up by the convenience of driving around <i>within</i> cities once you arrive. In addition, many of the country's major <b>natural attractions</b>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a>, are almost impossible to get to without an automobile or on a bus tour. If you have the time, a classic American road trip with a rented car is very easy to achieve. (You may have to shop around a bit for a one-way rental. Pay attention to how many miles they allow you to put on the vehicle, since you probably want to make detours for sightseeing.) Because of the distances, this kind of travel can mean many long days behind the wheel, so pay attention to the comfort of the car you use. A "coast-to-coast" trip with more than one driver and few stops will take at least 5 days (4½ if you have strong bladders).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Driving_laws">Driving laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Driving laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Driving law is primarily a matter of state law and is enforced by state and local police. While there are some minor variations state-to-state, the rules of the road are fairly consistent across the country. All states publish an official driver's handbook which summarizes state driving laws in plain English, with some states providing a Spanish version as well. These handbooks are usually available both on the Web and at many government offices. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.aaa.com">AAA</a> publishes a AAA/CAA Digest of Motor Laws, which is now <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://drivinglaws.aaa.com/">available online</a> for free that covers especially a few difference of traffic rules applied by all U.S. states and Canadian provinces.
</p><p>Foreign visitors age 18 and older can usually drive on their foreign driver's license for up to a year, depending on state law. Licenses that are not in English must be accompanied by an International Driving Permit (IDP) or a certified translation. Persons who stay in the United States for more than a year must obtain a driver's license from the state they are residing in, though exceptions might apply depending on the state they are in (e.g. some states waive this requirement for those on student visas). Written and practical driving tests are usually required, but they are sometimes waived for holders of some Canadian and European licenses.
</p><p>Americans drive on the right in left-hand drive vehicles, the same as Canada and Mexico.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG/250px-Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG/375px-Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG/500px-Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1959" data-file-height="1306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Street_East_Hartford_Connecticut_USA.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 5-light signal (left) indicating that both straight and left-turning traffic has the right of way. If just the green circle was displayed, without a green left arrow, left-turning traffic would have to yield to oncoming traffic.</div></div></div>
<p>Most American drivers tend to drive calmly and safely in residential neighborhoods. However, downtown surface streets and big-city expressways often become crowded with a lot of "hurried" drivers, who will exceed speed limits, make unsafe lane changes, or follow other cars at unsafe close distances ("tailgating"). Speed limits are variable depending on the area in which you are driving; enforcement is unpredictable and varies widely from state to state. Keeping pace with other drivers will usually avoid trouble. Beware of small towns along otherwise high-speed rural roads (and medium-speed suburban roads); the lower speed limits within these towns are strictly enforced.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_bus_2">By bus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: By bus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Long-distance_bus_travel_in_the_United_States" title="Long-distance bus travel in the United States">Long-distance bus travel in the United States</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>Intercity bus travel is widespread, but not available everywhere. Service between nearby major cities is extremely frequent, and often connect many smaller towns with regional cities. It's commonly considered a "lower class" way to travel, but is generally dependable, safe and affordable. However, bus stations in some cities are located in rough neighborhoods (e.g. Los Angeles).
</p><p><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.greyhound.com/">Greyhound Bus Lines</a></b> (<abbr title="phone" style="text-decoration:none">☏</abbr> <bdi class="tel listing-phone"><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="tel:+1-800-231-2222">+1-800-231-2222</a></span></bdi>) and several affiliated lines such as <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.boltbus.com">Bolt Bus</a></b> have the predominant share of American bus travel in 45 states. Discounts are available to travellers who purchase their tickets 7–14 days in advance of their travel date. Greyhound buses typically run in 5-7 hour segments, at which time all passengers must get off the bus so it can be serviced, even if it's the middle of the night.  Continuing passengers are boarded before those just getting on. Seating is on a first-come, first-served basis, except in some cities, where you can pay a $5 fee for priority seating.
</p><p><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://us.megabus.com">Megabus</a></b>, Greyhound's biggest competitor, operates mainly in 30 states in the Midwestern and the eastern half of the country between the hub cities of Atlanta, Chicago, Dallas, New Orleans, New York, Washington DC and several other cities surrounding and between the hubs. It also offers connections to Montreal and Toronto in Canada. It also has a couple of routes in the west, which are not connected to the other ones in the Midwest and the East Coast. 
</p><p><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.chinatown-bus.org">Chinatown buses</a></b> are small independent companies that provide curb-side departures for a cheap standard cash fare. These lines operate mainly in the northeast between Boston, New York City, Philadelphia, Washington DC, and Baltimore. Some continue further out to destinations in the Midwest and the South from the northeast. Others operate between California, Nevada and Arizona. See the relevant city guides and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.gotobus.com">GoToBus.com</a> for more information.
</p><p>Hispanic bus companies tend to have the most spacious buses in the country. Many are affiliate brands or subsidiaries of Mexican bus companies offering cross-border services beyond the border areas to as a far north as Chicago, to as far east as Atlanta and as far south as Mexico City. Connections from Texas hubs to the Midwest including Chicago, the Southeast and Mexico are offered by <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tornadobus.com/">Tornado Bus</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.elexpreso.net/">El Expreso</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.omnibusmexicanos.com.mx">Omnibus Mexicanos</a></b> and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.turimex.com/en/">Turimex Internacional</a></b>. Service in and out of Florida is offered by the Chilean <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jetsetusa.com/">JetSet</a></b>, Argentinian <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.redcoachusa.com/">RedCoach</a></b>, and Cuban-American <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.lacubanabus.com">La Cubana</a></b>. In California and the Southwest operators include <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://futuranet.com/">FuturaNet</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tufesa.com.mx">Tufesa</a></b>, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.intercalifornias.com">InterCalifornias</a></b> and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.eplalimo.com/">El Paso-Los Angeles Limousines</a></b>, which may have tickets starting from $1.
</p><p>Various smaller companies offer bus services throughout the country. A number of them are grouped under the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.trailways.com/">Trailways</a></b> brand, which you'll often find sharing space with Greyhound.
</p><p>All but the smallest cities and metro areas have some sort of <b>local bus service</b>, but it will often be limited compared to cities of a similar size in other countries. In general in the U.S., local buses don't stop at every stop. In some cities they'll stop if they see someone waiting at the bus stop, but in others you may have to give a little wave to let them know you want to get on. If you're on the bus and want to get off at the next stop, there's usually a button for you to push or a string to pull to signal the driver.
</p>
<h3><span id="By_recreational_vehicle_.28RV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="By_recreational_vehicle_(RV)">By recreational vehicle (RV)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: By recreational vehicle (RV)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/Car_camping" title="Car camping">Car camping</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>Recreational vehicles &#8211; large, sometimes bus-sized vehicles that include sleeping and living quarters &#8211; are a distinctly American way to cruise the country.  Some RVers love the convenience of being able to drive their home anywhere they like and enjoy the camaraderie that RV campgrounds offer.  Other people dislike the hassles and maintenance issues that come with RVing.  And don't even think about driving an RV into a huge metropolis such as New York.  Still, if you want to drive extensively within the United States and are comfortable handling a big rig, renting an RV is an option you should consider.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_motorcycle">By motorcycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: By motorcycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The thrill and exhilaration of cross country travel are magnified when you go by motorcycle. <b>Harley-Davidson</b> is the preeminent American motorcycle brand and Harley operates a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.harley-davidson.com/en_US/Content/Pages/Try_a_Bike/rent-a-bike.html?locale=en_US">motorcycle rental program</a> for those licensed and capable of handling a full weight motorcycle. In some parts of the country, you can also rent other types of motorcycles, such as sportbikes, touring bikes, and dual-sport bikes. For those inexperienced with motorcycles, Harley and other dealerships offer classes for beginners. Wearing a helmet, although not required in all states, is always a good idea. The practice of riding between lanes of slower cars, also known as "lane-sharing" or "lane-splitting," is illegal, except in California where it is tolerated and widespread. Solo motorcyclists can legally use "high-occupancy vehicle" or "carpool" lanes during their hours of operation.
</p><p>American enthusiasm towards motorcycles has led to a motorcycling subculture. <b>Motorcycle clubs</b> are exclusive clubs for members dedicated to riding a particular brand of motorcycle within a highly structured club hierarchy. <b>Riding clubs</b> may or may not be organized around a specific brand of bikes and offer open membership to anyone interested in riding.  Motorcycle rallies, such as the famous one in <a href="/wiki/Sturgis" title="Sturgis">Sturgis</a>, South Dakota, are huge gatherings of motorcyclists from around the country. Many motorcyclists are not affiliated with any club and opt to ride independently or with friends. In general, motorcycling is seen as a hobby, as opposed to a practical means of transportation; this means, for example, that most American motorcyclists prefer not to ride in inclement weather. However you choose to ride, and whatever brand of bike you prefer, motorcycling can be a thrilling way to see the country.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_thumb">By thumb</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: By thumb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg/250px-St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg/375px-St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg/500px-St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1929" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Louis_night_expblend.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gateway Arch, <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> in the Midwest</div></div></div>
<p>A long history of <a href="/wiki/Hitchhiking" title="Hitchhiking">hitchhiking</a> comes out of the U.S., with record of automobile hitchhikers as early as 1911. The specifics of the law vary from state to state, but in general, hitchhiking itself is legal throughout the majority of the country, though generally not on Interstate highways (where pedestrians are normally prohibited) or while standing within traffic lanes (usually marked by a solid white line at the shoulder of the road). If you plan to hitchhike, best practice is to thumb rides at entrance ramps, or (better yet) highway rest areas.
</p><p>However, due to increasing wariness of the possible dangers (fueled in part by sensational stories in the news media), hitchhiking in the U.S. is much less common than it used to be. International travelers to the U.S. should avoid this practice unless they have either a particularly strong sense of social adventure or extremely little money. Even many Americans themselves would only feel comfortable "thumbing a ride" if they had a good knowledge of the locale, and American drivers also practice caution for the same reasons.
</p><p><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://craigslist.org">Craigslist</a></b> has a rideshare section that sometimes proves useful for arranging rides in advance. If you are open with your destination it's almost always possible to find a ride going somewhere in the country, with payment often being sharing the fuel costs.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See">See</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: See">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg/250px-Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg/375px-Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg/500px-Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portland_Head_Light_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portland Head Lighthouse, <a href="/wiki/Portland_(Maine)" title="Portland (Maine)">Portland</a>, Maine, in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a></div></div></div>
<p>The United States is extraordinarily diverse in its array of attractions.  You will never run out of things to see; even if you think you've exhausted what one place has to offer, the next destination is only a road trip away.
</p><p>The <b>Great American Road Trip</b> (see <a href="#Great_American_Road_Trip">above</a>) is the most traditional way to see a variety of sights; just hop in the car and cruise down the Interstates, stopping at the convenient roadside hotels and restaurants as necessary, and stopping at every interesting tourist trap along the way, until you reach your destination.
</p><p>Indescribably beautiful scenery, history that reads like a screenplay, entertainment options that can last you for days, and some of the world's greatest architecture—no matter what your pleasure, you can find it almost anywhere you look in the United States.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_scenery">Natural scenery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Natural scenery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG/220px-Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG/330px-Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG/440px-Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordan_Pond_Maine.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jordan Pond in <a href="/wiki/Acadia_National_Park" title="Acadia National Park">Acadia National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a></div></div></div>
<p>From the spectacular glaciers of Alaska to the wooded, weathered peaks of Appalachia; from the otherworldly desertscapes of the Southwest to the vast waters of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>; few other countries have as wide a variety of natural scenery as the United States does.
</p><p>America's <b><a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Parks" title="United States National Parks">national parks</a></b> are a great place to start, and to see <a href="/wiki/North_American_wildlife" title="North American wildlife">North American wildlife</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a> was the first true national park in the world, and it remains one of the most famous, but there are 60 others. The Grand Canyon is possibly the world's most spectacular gorge; <a href="/wiki/Sequoia_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Sequoia National Park">Sequoia National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite National Park</a> are both home to the world's tallest living organisms; Glacier National Park is a great place to see huge sheets of ice; <a href="/wiki/Canyonlands_National_Park" title="Canyonlands National Park">Canyonlands National Park</a> could easily be mistaken for Mars; and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Smoky_Mountains_National_Park" title="Great Smoky Mountains National Park">Great Smoky Mountains National Park</a> features abundant wildlife among beautifully forested mountains. And the national parks aren't just for sightseeing, either; each has plenty of outdoors activities as well.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg/220px-Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg/330px-Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg/440px-Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carlsbad_Caverns_National_Park" title="Carlsbad Caverns National Park">Carlsbad Caverns National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlsbad_(New_Mexico)" title="Carlsbad (New Mexico)">Carlsbad</a>, New Mexico, in the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_(United_States_of_America)" title="Southwest (United States of America)">Southwest</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Still, the national parks are just the beginning.  The National Park Service also operates <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Monuments" title="United States National Monuments">National Monuments</a>, National Memorials, National Historic Sites, National Seashores, National Heritage Areas... <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Park_System" title="United States National Park System">the list goes on and on</a>.  And each state has its own <b>state parks</b> that can be just as good as the federal versions.  Most all of these destinations, federal or state, have an admission fee, but it all goes toward maintenance and operations of the parks, and the rewards are well worth it.
</p><p>Those aren't your only options, though.  Many of America's natural treasures can be seen without passing through admission gates.  The world-famous <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls_(New_York)" title="Niagara Falls (New York)">Niagara Falls</a> straddle the border between Canada and the U.S.; the American side lets you get right up next to the onrush and feel the power that has shaped the Niagara gorge.  The "purple majesty" of the Rocky Mountains can be seen for hundreds of miles in any direction, while the placid coastal areas of the Midwest and the Mid-Atlantic have relaxed Americans for generations.  And, although they are very different from each other, Hawaii and Alaska are perhaps the two most scenic states; they don't just <i>have</i> attractions&#8212;they <i>are</i> attractions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_attractions">Historical attractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Historical attractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taos_Pueblo_(5)_(15945350466).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Taos_Pueblo_%285%29_%2815945350466%29.jpg/220px-Taos_Pueblo_%285%29_%2815945350466%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="95" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Taos_Pueblo_%285%29_%2815945350466%29.jpg/330px-Taos_Pueblo_%285%29_%2815945350466%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Taos_Pueblo_%285%29_%2815945350466%29.jpg/440px-Taos_Pueblo_%285%29_%2815945350466%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="1735" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taos_Pueblo_(5)_(15945350466).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Taos_Pueblo" title="Taos Pueblo">Taos Pueblo</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_List" title="UNESCO World Heritage List">UNESCO World Heritage site</a>, and one of the largest pre-Columbian settlements in the southwest.</div></div></div>
<p>The U.S. has a tremendous wealth of historical attractions—more than enough to fill months of history-centric touring.
</p><p>The <b>prehistory</b> of the continent can indeed be a little hard to uncover, as most of the Native American tribes did not build permanent settlements. But particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains_(United_States_of_America)" title="Rocky Mountains (United States of America)">West</a>, you will find magnificent cliff dwellings at sites such as <a href="/wiki/Mesa_Verde_National_Park" title="Mesa Verde National Park">Mesa Verde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bandelier_National_Monument" title="Bandelier National Monument">Bandelier</a>, as well as near-ubiquitous rock paintings (Petroglyph National Park has some of the best rock art in the country, and it is located only 17 km outside of <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque" title="Albuquerque">Albuquerque</a>). The Museum of the American Indian in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C./National_Mall" title="Washington, D.C./National Mall">Washington, D.C.</a> is another great place to start learning about America's culture before the arrival of Europeans colonists.
</p><p>The first successful British colony on the continent was at <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Williamsburg" title="Colonial Williamsburg">Jamestown</a>, Virginia, although the settlement at <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_(Massachusetts)" title="Plymouth (Massachusetts)">Plymouth</a>, Massachusetts, may loom larger in the nation's mind. The eastern states of New England, the Mid-Atlantic, and the South have more than their fair share of sites from <b>early American history</b> as they make up what are known as the 13 Original Colonies. 
</p><p>In the 18th century, major centers of commerce developed in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, and as the colonies grew in size, wealth, and self-confidence, relations with Great Britain became strained, culminating in the Boston Tea Party and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Early_United_States_history" title="Early United States history">Revolutionary War</a>.
</p><p>There are a large number of historic sites related to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, the most destructive conflict on American soil.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monuments_and_architecture">Monuments and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Monuments and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Americans have never shied away from heroic feats of engineering, and many of them are among the country's biggest tourist attractions.
</p><p><b>Washington, D.C.</b>, as the nation's capital, has more monuments and statuary than you could see in a day, but do be sure to visit the Washington Monument (the world's tallest obelisk), the stately Lincoln Memorial, and the incredibly moving Vietnam Veterans Memorial.  The city's architecture is also an attraction&#8212;the Capitol Building and the White House are two of the most iconic buildings in the country and often serve to represent the whole nation to the world.
</p><p>A number of American cities have world-renowned skylines, perhaps none more so than the concrete canyons of <b>Manhattan</b>, part of New York City.  There, a new World Trade Center tower has risen on a site adjacent to the fallen twin towers, and the Empire State Building and the Chrysler Building still stand tall, as they have for almost a century.  <b>Chicago</b>, where the skyscraper was invented, can no longer claim the <i>tallest</i> building in the country, but it still has an awful lot of <a href="/wiki/Chicago_skyline_guide" title="Chicago skyline guide"><i>really tall</i> buildings</a>.  Other skylines worth seeing include San Francisco (with the Golden Gate Bridge), Seattle (including the Space Needle), Miami, and <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LincolnsTomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/LincolnsTomb.jpg/220px-LincolnsTomb.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/LincolnsTomb.jpg/330px-LincolnsTomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/LincolnsTomb.jpg/440px-LincolnsTomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="819" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LincolnsTomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Abraham Lincoln's tomb, in <a href="/wiki/Springfield_(Illinois)" title="Springfield (Illinois)">Springfield, Illinois</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some human constructions transcend skyline, though, and become iconic symbols in their own right.  The Gateway Arch in St. Louis, the Statue of Liberty in Manhattan, the Hollywood Sign in Los Angeles, and even the fountains of the Bellagio casino in Las Vegas all draw visitors to their respective cities.  Even the incredible <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rushmore" class="mw-redirect" title="Mount Rushmore">Mount Rushmore</a>, located far from any major city, still attracts two million visitors each year.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_and_galleries">Museums and galleries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Museums and galleries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the U.S., there's a <a href="/wiki/Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Museum">museum</a> for <i>practically everything</i>.  From toys to priceless artifacts, from entertainment legends to dinosaur bones&#8212;nearly every city in the country has a museum worth visiting.
</p><p>The highest concentrations of these museums are found in the largest cities, of course, but none compare to Washington, D.C., home to the <b>Smithsonian Institution</b>.  With almost twenty independent museums, most of them located on the <b><a href="/wiki/National_Mall" class="mw-redirect" title="National Mall">National Mall</a></b>, the Smithsonian is the foremost curator of American history and achievement.  The most popular of the Smithsonian museums are the <b>National Air and Space Museum</b>, the <b>National Museum of American History</b>, and the <b>National Museum of Natural History</b>, but any of the Smithsonian museums would be a great way to spend an afternoon&#8212;and they're all 100% free.
</p><p>New York City also has an outstanding array of world-class museums, including the <b>Guggenheim Museum</b>, the <b>American Museum of Natural History</b>,the <b>Museum of Modern Art (MOMA)</b>, the <b>Metropolitan Museum of Art</b>, the <b>Intrepid Sea-Air-Space Museum</b>, and the <b>Ellis Island Immigration Museum</b>.
</p><p>You could spend weeks exploring the cultural institutions just in D.C. and the Big Apple, but there are also many other cities with world class museums such as <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>. Many universities also operate small museums that have interesting exhibits and are often free to enter, while those interested in specific sports or topics will often be able to find museums even in some small towns that suit their tastes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Itineraries">Itineraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Itineraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Here is a handful of itineraries spanning regions across the United States:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Trail" title="Appalachian Trail">Appalachian Trail</a> &#8212; a foot trail along the spine of the Appalachian Mountains from Georgia to Maine</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Braddock_Expedition" title="Braddock Expedition">Braddock Expedition</a> &#8212; traces the French-Indian War route of British General Edward Braddock (and a younger George Washington) from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_(Virginia)" title="Alexandria (Virginia)">Alexandria</a>, Virginia through Cumberland, Maryland to the Monongahela River near Pittsburgh</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Interstate_5" title="Interstate 5">Interstate 5</a> &#8212; the primary interstate highway along the west coast from the Mexican border with California to the Canadian border with Washington state, passing through the major west coast cities and the capitals of three states</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Jazz_Track" title="The Jazz Track">The Jazz Track</a> — a nation-wide tour of the most important clubs in jazz history and in jazz performance today</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Trail" title="Lewis and Clark Trail">Lewis and Clark Trail</a> &#8212; retrace the northwest route of the great American explorers along the Missouri River</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oregon_Trail" title="Oregon Trail">Oregon Trail</a> &#8212; the mid-19th century path taken by western settlers from Missouri to Oregon</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Crest_Trail" title="Pacific Crest Trail">Pacific Crest Trail</a> &#8212; one of the original National Scenic Trails; travels along the west coast from Mexico to Canada</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pony_Express_National_Historic_Trail" title="Pony Express National Historic Trail">Pony Express National Historic Trail</a> &#8212; commemorates the mail service route through the Old West, between St. Joseph, Missouri and San Francisco, California</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Route_66" title="Route 66">Route 66</a> &#8212; tour the iconic historic highway running from Chicago to Los Angeles</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_Trail" title="Santa Fe Trail">Santa Fe Trail</a> &#8212; a historic southwest settler route from <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> to <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_(New_Mexico)" title="Santa Fe (New Mexico)">Santa Fe</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Touring_Shaker_country" title="Touring Shaker country">Touring Shaker country</a> — takes you to one current and eight former Shaker religious communities in the Mid-Atlantic, New England and Midwest regions of the United States</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trail_Of_Tears_National_Historic_Trail" class="mw-redirect" title="Trail Of Tears National Historic Trail">Trail Of Tears National Historic Trail</a> &#8212; follows the route of the forced westward migration of many American Indian tribes in the 1830s</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_1" title="U.S. Highway 1">U.S. Highway 1</a> &#8212; travelling along the east coast from Maine to Florida</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Do">Do</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Do">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_music">Arts and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Arts and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mid-size to large cities often draw big ticket <b>concerts</b>, especially in large outdoor amphitheaters. Small towns sometimes host concerts in parks with local or older bands. Other options include music festivals such has <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a>'s <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.street-scene.com/">Street Scene</a></b> or <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sxsw.com">South by Southwest</a></b> in <a href="/wiki/Austin" title="Austin">Austin</a>. <b><a href="/wiki/Classical_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical music">Classical music</a></b> concerts are held year round and performed by semi-professional and professional symphonies. Many cities and regions have unique sounds. <b>Country music</b> is popular throughout the U.S. but is particularly concentrated in the South and rural West. <a href="/wiki/Nashville" title="Nashville">Nashville</a> is known as "Music City" because of the large number of country artists who live in the city. It's home to the <b>Grand Ole Opry</b>, one of the most famous music venues in the country, and numerous other live music venues. African-Americans in the South gave rise to <b><a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a></b> and the <b>blues</b>, with numerous music scenes and venues in cities nationwide. Many of the most popular mainstream bands are based in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> due to the large entertainment presence and concentration of record companies.
</p><p>America is considered to be the spiritual home of <b><a href="/wiki/Musical_theater" class="mw-redirect" title="Musical theater">musical theater</a></b>, and many of the world's most famous musicals have had a run on Broadway in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> at one time or another. No trip to New York would be complete without catching at least one musical on Broadway. The United States is also home to one of the world's premier <b>opera</b> companies, the Metropolitan Opera in New York.
</p><p>A quintessential American experience is the <b>marching band</b> festival. You can find these events almost every weekend between September and Thanksgiving throughout the country and again from March to June in California. Check local event listings and papers to find specifics. Also notable is the Bands of America Grand National Championship held every autumn in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a>. To see the best of the best, get tickets to the "finals" performance, where the ten best bands of the festival compete for the championship. Both "street" or parade marching bands as well as "field" or show bands are found at almost every high school and university in America.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg/250px-Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg/375px-Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg/500px-Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1196" data-file-height="864" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackierobinsonstadium1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baseball in <a href="/wiki/Daytona_Beach" title="Daytona Beach">Daytona Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a></div></div></div>
<p>The United States has a professional league for virtually every sport, including pillow fighting. America's passion for sports is rivaled hardly anywhere in the world, with the leagues with the world's highest attendance both per game (NFL) and total (MLB) and other leagues that are the best and most popular in their respective sport. Watching a game is a good way to meet and interact with the locals. A few of the most popular sports are:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_the_United_States" title="Baseball in the United States">Baseball</a></b>, often referred to as "America's pastime", is one of the most widely played sports in the country. The U.S. is home to 29 of the 30 <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.mlb.com">MLB</a></b> (Major League Baseball) teams. The season lasts from April to September with playoff games held in October, with the championship games known as the <b>World Series</b>. With each team playing 162 games per team per season and the cheapest seats usually $10-20, this is possibly the best sporting event for international travellers to watch. There are also several hundred minor league teams scattered across the U.S.; while quality of the games is lower, prices are cheaper (even free in a few leagues).</li>
<li>The U.S. is home to 29 of the 30 <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nba.com">NBA</a></b> (National Basketball Association) teams, and the world's premier men's <b><a href="/wiki/Basketball_in_North_America" title="Basketball in North America">basketball</a></b> league. The season runs from November to April, with playoffs in May–June. Its counterpart the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.wnba.com">WNBA</a></b> (Women's NBA), which plays during the NBA offseason, is one of the most stable and popular women's team sports leagues in the world.</li>
<li>The <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nfl.com">NFL</a></b> (National Football League), with 32 teams (all in the contiguous U.S.) is the leading promoter of <b><a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a></b> in the world. It has virtually nothing in common with association football (Americans know <i>that</i> sport as soccer). It developed from <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">rugby football</a>, and still has some things in common with its cousin from England. It is extremely popular, and the day of the championship game, the <b>Super Bowl</b>, is an unofficial national holiday and perennially the most watched event in American sports. The season lasts from September to December, with playoffs in January ending with the Super Bowl in February.</li></ul>
<table class="pp_infobox" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left: 1em; width:40%;" cellpadding="8" cellspacing="1">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="background-color:#effafa; font-size:90%; border:1px solid #ccc; padding-left: 1em; padding-right: 1em; text-align:left; width:100%;">
<p><b>"Hockey" vs "Ice hockey"</b>
</p><p>In most English-speaking countries, "hockey" is used for a game played on grass and "ice hockey" for the one on ice. In North American usage, however, the former is called "field hockey", while "hockey" alone almost always means "ice hockey" (or, rarely, roller hockey).
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li>The <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nhl.com">NHL</a></b> (National Hockey League) is the premier <b><a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_in_North_America" title="Ice hockey in North America">ice hockey</a></b> league in the world. 24 of its 31 teams are in the U.S., with another U.S. team to be added in 2021. Slightly under 50% of players are Canadians, another 25% Americans, and the rest come from many other parts of the world, mainly northern and eastern Europe. The season runs from October to April, followed by playoffs that culminate in the <b>Stanley Cup Finals</b> in June, the titular cup of which is the oldest professional sports trophy in North America.</li>
<li>Beginning as the original form of American motorsport in 1911 with the first Indianapolis 500, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.indycar.com">IndyCar</a></b> has since come to be the premier <b>open-wheel racing</b> series in North America. The competition in IndyCar is known to be closer, faster, and far more dangerous than that of NASCAR. Unlike NASCAR which almost exclusively races on "oval" tracks, IndyCar competes on a wide variety of tracks ranging from city streets, road courses, to ovals like the Indianapolis Motor Speedway which plays host to the <b>Indianapolis 500</b>, where speeds can reach up to 240 mph (385 km/h)! IndyCar holds races all across the United States from March to October.</li>
<li>Viewed by many as a "regional sport" confined to the more rural areas of the South, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nascar.com">NASCAR</a></b> (National Association for Stock Car Auto Racing) has seemingly broken away from those misconceptions to become a major spectator sport across the country. While a majority of the tracks still reside in the Mid-Atlantic and South, NASCAR holds races all across the country, beginning with their marquee event, the <b>Daytona 500</b>, in mid-February and ending in late November.</li>
<li>As of the 2020 season, <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.mlssoccer.com">MLS</a></b> (Major League <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Soccer</a>) has 26 teams—23 in the U.S. plus three in Canada—with four more U.S. teams to be added by 2022. While it may not be as popular with the media, MLS is still widely viewed and enjoyed (particularly by Hispanic communities), and is a preferred destination for top players from European leagues who are past their prime. The season does not coincide with soccer in most other countries: the regular season runs March to October, with MLS Cup playoffs from October to December. The women's equivalent is the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://nwslsoccer.com/">National Women's Soccer League (NWSL)</a></b>, which has 9 teams, all in the US, with another US-based team confirmed to be joining in 2021 and plans to expand to 12 or 14 teams in the early 2020s.</li></ul>
<p>One unique feature of the American sports landscape is the extent to which sports are associated with educational institutions. In many regions, especially the South and Midwest, <b>college sports</b> enjoy followings that rival or surpass those of major professional teams, especially football and men's basketball. (In fact, 8 of the 10 largest non-motorsports stadiums in the world — all seating more than 100,000 spectators — are for U.S. college football teams, and the country's three largest basketball arenas house college teams.) The <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ncaa.com">NCAA</a></b> (National Collegiate Athletic Association) has over 1,000 member schools, including almost all of the country's best-known colleges and universities. The <b>college football</b> and <b>college basketball</b> seasons roughly coincide with their professional counterparts; the NCAA Division I men's basketball playoff tournament, <b>"March Madness"</b>, is especially widely followed even by casual sports fans. <b>Rowing</b> enthusiasts may wish to watch the <b>Harvard–Yale Regatta</b>, a 4-mile-long (6.4&#160;km) race held in <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a> every year between the men's coxed eight rowing teams.
</p><p>Many communities also take great pride in their <b>high school sports</b> teams, and especially in smaller locales, those teams are a major part of local culture. From August to May, a high school game can be a great (and cheap) way to meet locals and discover the area in a way many visitors never experience. The most popular sports are usually football and boys' basketball (and to a lesser extent girls' basketball), plus hockey in New England and the upper Midwest. In some areas, a particular high school sport enjoys an elevated cultural position. Examples include football in Texas, basketball in Indiana, hockey in Minnesota, and wrestling in Iowa.
</p><p>The United States is home to many of the world's most famous <b>golf</b> courses. The most famous is the <a href="/wiki/Augusta_(Georgia)" title="Augusta (Georgia)">Augusta</a> National Golf Club, which is the home of the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.masters.com/index.html">Masters</a></b>, one of the world's most prestigious professional golf tournaments, and also one of the four majors in men's golf. The U.S. is also home to 2 of the other 3 majors in men's golf, namely the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://usga.usopen.com/">U.S. Open</a></b> and the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pga.com/pgachampionship/">PGA Championship</a></b>, which rotate between different golf courses in the U.S. every year. Golf is popular both as a participation and spectator sport, and the U.S. supports several major professional tours. See also <a href="/wiki/Golf#United_States_of_America" title="Golf">Golf#United States of America</a>.
</p><p>The United States hosts many <b>tennis</b> tournaments in the ATP and WTA tours, with the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.usopen.org/index.html">US Open</a></b> being the most prestigious among them. It is regarded as one of the four Grand Slams. The US Open is held every year from late August to Early September at the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ustanew2.gotennissource.com/">USTA Billie Jean King National Tennis Center</a></b> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.
</p><p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Rodeo_(event)" title="Rodeo (event)">rodeo</a></b> celebrates the traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Old_West" title="Old West">Old West</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a>. A subset of rodeo, bull riding, enjoys a moderate degree of popularity as a standalone event, with the main circuit being <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.pbr.com">Professional Bull Riders</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals_and_fairs">Festivals and fairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Festivals and fairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg/220px-2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg/330px-2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg/440px-2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2006_Iowa_State_Fair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Iowa State Fair, one of the most well-known state fairs. Common sights at state fairs include food stalls, amusement rides, and local booths selling goods.</div></div></div>
<p>Many towns and/or counties throw <b>fairs</b> to commemorate the establishment of a town or the county with rides, games, and other attractions. Almost every state has one or more <b>state fairs</b>. These began as competitions and shows to promote agriculture and livestock; now they include industrial product exhibitions, concerts, and carnival rides and games.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature">Nature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Nature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are numerous <b><a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Parks" title="United States National Parks">national parks</a></b> throughout the United States, especially the vast interior, which offer plenty of opportunities to enjoy outdoor activities, including <a href="/wiki/Recreational_shooting" title="Recreational shooting">Recreational shooting</a>, ATV riding, hiking, bird watching, prospecting, and horseback riding. National parks are the crown jewels of the much larger <b><a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Park_System" title="United States National Park System">National Park System</a></b>, which also includes historic and cultural landmarks.
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/National_Trails_System" class="mw-redirect" title="National Trails System">National Trails System</a></b> is a group of 21 "National Scenic Trails" and "National Historic Trails", and over 1,000 shorter "National Recreation Trails" for a total length of over 50,000&#160;mi (80,000&#160;km). While all are open to hiking, most are also open to mountain biking, horseback riding, and camping and some are open for ATVs and cars.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amusement_parks">Amusement parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Amusement parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The United States is the birthplace of the modern <a href="/wiki/Amusement_parks" title="Amusement parks">amusement park</a>, and to this day, amusement parks form an integral part of American childhood and teenage culture. The first ever permanent amusement park was built on <a href="/wiki/Coney_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Coney Island">Coney Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, and while not as glamorous as some of the newer ones, is still home to a famous historic wooden roller coaster and numerous other attractions.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orlando" title="Orlando">Orlando</a> areas in particular are home to numerous well-regarded amusement parks, with giants <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.universalparks.com/">Universal</a></b> and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://disneyparks.disney.go.com/">Disney</a></b> operating parks in both locations. Another chain of amusement parks that is well-regarded locally, though not so well-known internationally, is <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.sixflags.com/">Six Flags</a></b>, which has multiple locations throughout the country, and is particularly known for its innovative roller coasters and other thrill rides. Other chains include the marine-themed <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://seaworldparks.com/">SeaWorld</a></b>, which is known for its marine mammal shows, and <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.cedarfair.com">Cedar Fair</a></b>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Buy">Buy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Buy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Money">Money</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Money">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USDnotesNew.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/USDnotesNew.png/350px-USDnotesNew.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/USDnotesNew.png/525px-USDnotesNew.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/USDnotesNew.png/700px-USDnotesNew.png 2x" data-file-width="2380" data-file-height="2075" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USDnotesNew.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 2018 series of U.S. $100, $50, $20, $10, $5, $2 and $1 bills. Older styles are still frequently seen in circulation.</div></div></div>
<p>The official U.S. currency is the <b>United States dollar</b> (<b>$</b>), divided into 100 <b>cents</b> (<b>¢</b>, but often written as decimal dollars). Foreign currencies are almost never accepted, though some major hotel chains may accept traveller's checks in other currencies. Establishments close to the Canadian border accept Canadian currency, though usually at poor exchange rates. The Mexican peso can also be used (again at poor exchange rates) in border towns like <a href="/wiki/El_Paso" title="El Paso">El Paso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laredo" title="Laredo">Laredo</a>.
