Unless you are calling from a mobile or Internet-based phone, the operator should be able to locate you from the phone you are using even if you do not say anything.  Modern cell phones will send a GPS fix of your location down to a few meters within a few seconds of dialing 911. Dialing 911 and leaving an open line will bring all 3 emergency services to your location in under 5 minutes in most populated areas. Response time may be longer in sparsely populated areas or along the Interstates.
</p><p>On any GSM mobile phone (the standard technology in most of the world, especially in Europe), you can also dial <b>112</b>, which is the standard emergency number for GSM networks worldwide. U.S. GSM carriers (AT&amp;T, T-Mobile, and smaller regional operators) automatically redirect 112 calls to 911.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Border_Patrol">Border Patrol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=83" title="Edit section: Border Patrol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The United States Border Patrol works near both the Canadian and the Mexican borders, as well as in Southern coastal areas like the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Keys" title="Florida Keys">Florida Keys</a>. They can verify immigration status and enforce immigration laws in the "border zones"—generally within 40 miles of Canada and 75 miles of Mexico (although the law allows for 100 miles from any border, including sea and the Great Lakes). Near Canada they tend to be unobtrusive and generally focus their work on long-distance buses and trains. Near the southern border, systematic vehicle checkpoints or being stopped on the street with a friendly "Papers, please..." is much more likely. They tend not to target tourists specifically.
</p><p>Foreigners are always required to carry their passports, visas, and landing cards (or Green Cards). Being found without them near the border could lead you to being detained until your status is verified, or possibly fined. If your documents are in order, you generally won't be questioned. In most states (<a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> is a notable exception), police and other local authorities are not allowed to question you about your immigration status or to ask to see your passport or visa unless you're arrested and charged with a crime, and then only for the purpose of connecting you with your embassy. As a result of the 9/11 attacks, some statistics have shown that Muslims or those who are assumed to be Muslims may be disproportionately targeted for additional screenings at airports despite claims that passengers are chosen at random.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_disasters">Natural disasters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=84" title="Edit section: Natural disasters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg/220px-Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg/330px-Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg/440px-Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1848" data-file-height="1228" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfalfa_Tornado_-_NOAA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tornadoes are more common in the United States than in any other country</div></div></div>
<p>The U.S. is a huge country with very varied geography, and parts of it are occasionally affected by natural disasters: <b>hurricanes</b> and <b>tropical storms</b> from June through November in the <a href="/wiki/South_(United_States_of_America)" title="South (United States of America)">South</a> and along the East Coast, <b>blizzards</b> in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, near the Great Lakes, and in the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains_(United_States_of_America)" title="Rocky Mountains (United States of America)">Rocky Mountains</a>, <b>tornadoes</b> mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> and Midwest, <b>earthquakes</b> on the West Coast and in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <b>floods</b> in areas of the Midwest and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and <b>wildfires</b> in the late summer and early fall in the western half, particularly <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_(United_States_of_America)" title="Southwest (United States of America)">Southwest</a>. See the regions in question for more details.
</p><p>Because tornadoes are so common between the Rocky Mountains and the Appalachian Mountains, this area has earned itself the colloquial name <b>Tornado Alley</b>. Hawaii contains several active volcanoes, but they are not usually a threat to life and limb. The last high profile eruption in the mainland U.S. was that of Mount Saint Helens in 1980.
</p><p>In the case of a natural disaster, local, state or federal authorities can issue a warning over the Emergency Alert System. It has a very distinctive electronic screeching followed by a sound similar to a dial tone prior to any message. It will override AM/FM radio broadcasts as well as TV systems. Smartphones sold since about 2011 will often receive an alert message based on the current location of the phone (depending on the phone's settings, this may include a loud alert tone). Coast Guard weather is broadcast continuously on VHF marine radio for <a href="/wiki/Cruising_in_small_craft" class="mw-redirect" title="Cruising in small craft">seafarers</a>; a separate system (seven frequencies around 161 MHz) provides conditions ashore. Special "weather radios" are able to monitor the frequency, even in standby mode, and sound the alarm if deadly storms (such as tornadoes or hurricanes) are brewing. In most tornado-prone regions, a system of sirens will sound when a tornado warning is issued. If you hear the siren, seek shelter immediately. (For more information, see <i><a href="/wiki/Tornado_safety" title="Tornado safety">tornado safety</a></i>.)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gay_and_lesbian">Gay and lesbian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=85" title="Edit section: Gay and lesbian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG/220px-Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG/330px-Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG/440px-Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castro_Street_Pedcrossing_with_Rainbow_Flag_Colour.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco/Castro-Noe_Valley" title="San Francisco/Castro-Noe Valley">Castro Street</a> pedestrian crosswalk with Rainbow Flag Colors</div></div></div>
<p>In general, the U.S. is a safe destination for gay and lesbian travelers, though as a whole, homosexuality is not quite as well accepted as in Australia, New Zealand, Canada or Western Europe. Most Americans take a live-and-let-live approach to sexuality, and anti-gay violence is very uncommon (though not totally unheard of), but you may receive unwanted attention or remarks in some situations or regions. In general, acceptance of homosexuality is most widespread (to a degree generally on par with Western Europe) in larger cities, college towns, in the Northeast and along the West Coast. However, this is just a general rule of thumb: you'll find homophobes (and, conversely, LGBT-accepting folks) in all corners of the country.
</p><p>The <b>rainbow flag</b> or <b>gay pride flag</b> is widely known even outside the gay community, and is commonly used by both individuals and businesses to signal that they (as individuals) are LGBT themselves or tolerant thereof, or that they (as businesses) are LGBT-owned and/or welcoming to LGBT customers. Other symbols (such as the pink triangle, or specific flags for bisexual or transgender people) exist but are much less widely recognized.
</p><p>Gay-friendly destinations include <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Manhattan/Chelsea" title="Manhattan/Chelsea">Chelsea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austin" title="Austin">Austin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Chicago/Lakeview-North_Center" title="Chicago/Lakeview-North Center">Boystown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Seattle/Capitol_Hill-Central_District" title="Seattle/Capitol Hill-Central District">Capitol Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>'s <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco/Castro-Noe_Valley" title="San Francisco/Castro-Noe Valley">Castro Street</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Washington_(D.C.)/Dupont_Circle" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington (D.C.)/Dupont Circle">Dupont Circle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miami_Beach" title="Miami Beach">Miami Beach</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Miami/South_Beach" class="mw-redirect" title="Miami/South Beach">South Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlanta/Midtown" title="Atlanta/Midtown">Midtown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>' <a href="/wiki/West_Hollywood" title="West Hollywood">West Hollywood</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. An increasing number of resort areas, too, are gay-friendly, including <a href="/wiki/Fire_Island" title="Fire Island">Fire Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Key_West" title="Key West">Key West</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asheville" title="Asheville">Asheville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provincetown" title="Provincetown">Provincetown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogunquit" title="Ogunquit">Ogunquit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rehoboth_Beach" title="Rehoboth Beach">Rehoboth Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saugatuck-Douglas" class="mw-redirect" title="Saugatuck-Douglas">Saugatuck</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asbury_Park" title="Asbury Park">Asbury Park</a>. Even leaving aside major tourist destinations, most cities have specific neighborhoods where gay people tend to congregate, and many have resource centers for LGBT people.
</p><p>If you're married to someone of the same sex, you may encounter some difficulties in more conservative areas of the country, but Supreme Court rulings have made it clear that no municipal, state or federal authority is allowed to treat same-sex relationships differently from opposite-sex ones. In most jurisdictions, individual businesses remain free to refuse service to gays and lesbians; fewer than twenty states list sexual orientation as a protected category (like race and gender) and fewer still extend these protections to transgender persons. While <b>transgender</b> persons are not prohibited from travel, some have reported undue scrutiny at airport security checkpoints. A few large cities have enacted local anti-discrimination ordinances, and many places have alternative monthly or weekly <b>publications</b> providing LGBT-relevant news and event listings. National LGBT publications include <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.out.com/">Out</a></i> magazine and <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.theadvocate.com/">The Advocate</a></i>.
</p><p>Men planning to engage in any sexual activity should be aware of the heightened risk of HIV and other infections in the United States. A gay American man is 44 times more likely to contract HIV than a heterosexual one, and 46 times more likely to contract syphilis. This risk grows greatly among men likely to engage in one-night stands and other higher-risk behavior. In a nation where 0.5% of the population are infected with HIV, unprotected sex is a very real risk. Precautions, including using protection, are strongly advised during your stay. Most cities have affordable or free testing and treatment centers for STIs, though hours may be limited and waits may be long. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.plannedparenthood.org/">Planned Parenthood</a> clinics are often an affordable alternative. Seeking healthcare elsewhere can be very pricey.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drugs">Drugs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=86" title="Edit section: Drugs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In general U.S. drug laws can be pretty severe: even possession or transportation of small amounts can lead to prison or deportation. However, laws and attitudes concerning the most commonly available drug, <b><a href="/wiki/Cannabis" title="Cannabis">marijuana</a></b>, vary wildly from state to state. States like Louisiana and Florida impose large fines and lengthy prison sentences, while in other states marijuana use has been largely decriminalized. 18 states allow medical use of marijuana, where persons can obtain marijuana for medicinal use with a doctor's prescription and a "medical marijuana card". In some states, particularly cities on the West Coast, medical marijuana dispensaries are so commonplace that they seem almost ordinary. <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> allow limited recreational use of marijuana, as does the <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>, although the status of legalization there is in doubt due to the District's unique Federal status. Under no circumstances should you transport marijuana or other drugs that are illegal under federal law across state lines (even when it's legal on both sides of the state line), onto Indian reservations, onto federal lands or properties (such as federal office buildings, military bases, and post offices), on flights or across international borders. Doing so is considered drug trafficking and subject to the aforementioned harsh penalties. 
</p>
<div style="display:table; align:center; background-color:#ffffdf; border:1px solid #888822; margin:0.5em 1em 0 1em; padding:6px;" class="pp_cautionbox">
<table>

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><img alt="Caution" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/40px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Caution" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/60px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg/80px-Exclamationdiamond_blue.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" />
</td>
<td><span style="font-size:130%; color:#000000;"><b>Note:</b></span> You can be turned away from the U.S. border if you are carrying any substance prohibited by U.S. federal laws, including marijuana. You can also be turned away from the U.S. border if you are employed in the cannabis industry where this is a legal activity. You can be banned from entering the U.S. for life if you lie to a customs official. Actually <i>crossing</i> the U.S. border with any quantity of cannabis in any form is a federal offense.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prostitution">Prostitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=87" title="Edit section: Prostitution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prostitution is illegal except at licensed brothels in rural <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>. Tolerance varies considerably between states. Police officers occasionally pose as prostitutes to catch and arrest anyone offering to pay for sex.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guns">Guns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=88" title="Edit section: Guns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5760" data-file-height="3840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:No_Fun_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"No recreational shooting", Roxborough State Park, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a></div></div></div>
<p>It's true: the U.S. has a strong <b>gun culture</b>, and many (but by no means all) Americans own a firearm. Possession of firearms is regulated by individual states, and while these regulations (obtaining necessary permits, the kinds of arms permitted) vary greatly from state to state and, sometimes, from city to city within the same state, the U.S. is generally considered to be a place with lenient attitudes towards firearm ownership, especially compared to Europe and Asia.
</p><p>Although U.S. citizens have a constitutionally guaranteed right to own and carry firearms, non-immigrant aliens present in America for fewer than 180 days cannot legally possess a firearm or ammunition, unless they travelled specifically for hunting or sport shooting, <i>or</i> they have a valid hunting license from the state they are shooting in. Entry in a recognized shooting competition also qualifies. Anything else is strictly illegal.
</p><p><b>Warning:</b> People who have renounced U.S. citizenship are not allowed to possess firearms or ammunition, even for sporting purposes.
</p><p>Your chances of getting shot are <b>very low</b>, but bear in mind that:
</p>
<ul><li>In a city, a civilian with an openly visible firearm is generally a rare sight, and thus potentially more of a concern than one in the country. Nonetheless, since many states do permit "open carry", you may encounter somebody with a holstered firearm. Police officers, even detectives who wear civilian clothes on duty, will almost always carry firearms. Many states also have "concealed carry" laws which permit the possession of a concealed firearm in clothing or in a vehicle. Keep in mind that people with permits to carry a firearm, openly or concealed, are usually not criminals and not going to harm you.</li>
<li>Hunting is popular in rural America. Use of marked trails is generally safe, but if you plan to venture off the beaten path, find out where any hunting may be afoot. If so, everyone in your party (including your dog!) should wear bright colors, particularly "Blaze Orange", to be highly visible to the hunters. The timing and length of hunting seasons, and any applicable permits and regulations, vary between states—see respective state government websites for more information. Hunting is not normally allowed in national or state parks, but is permitted in some national forests.</li>
<li>Target shooting is a popular sport. Many ranges welcome tourists and will have a variety of firearms available to rent and shoot at the range. Many implement a "two person minimum" rule and consider it unsafe to rent firearms to lone individuals.</li>
<li>The legal carrying of firearms for protection by individuals hiking, exploring or camping in the wilderness is on the rise due to a small number of highly publicized incidents along well-known hiking trails.  This is a controversial issue in the hiking/camping community, with strong arguments on both sides.  The proponents argue that legal possession of a firearm does not increase the level of danger for bystanders: those who carry may very well have military or police backgrounds and be more than willing to assist others in an emergency.</li>
<li><b>Private property</b> is more strongly protected in the U.S., both in law and in custom, than it is in many other parts of the world. In some areas, it is legal for owners to shoot people in defense of their property. While such incidents are rare, don't risk it - make sure you avoid taking shortcuts across land that might be privately owned, even if unfenced. In all cases, it's considered to be trespassing, which is a crime.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racism">Racism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=89" title="Edit section: Racism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Compared to many European and Asian countries, the U.S. is, at least publicly, a racially tolerant country. The U.S. Constitution, coupled with both state and federal legislation and case law, prohibits racial discrimination in a range of public spheres such as employment, university admissions and receiving services from retail businesses. However, the Constitution also guarantees freedom of speech to a greater degree than in most other Western democracies, so it is unfortunately possible to encounter racist comments (both blatant and subtle) in public forums. 
</p><p>Still, most Americans are, or at least profess to be, tolerant of other races, and it is rare to face open aggression from random people solely as a result of one's race. The country goes through occasional periods of increased animosity toward racial minorities or immigrants, but the longer-term trend has been one of increasing tolerance and acceptance.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stay_healthy">Stay healthy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=90" title="Edit section: Stay healthy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Tap_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Tap water">Tap water</a></b> is potable, but sometimes avoided due to the taste imparted by the chlorine used to purify it. It's of good quality in many places, including <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>; however, it tastes bad in certain low-rainfall areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Santa_Barbara" title="Santa Barbara">Santa Barbara</a>, so you might prefer to drink bottled water or seltzer in those places. Bottled water is near ubiquitous and available for a variety of prices. Quality tends to be high and the chemical difference between a 50 cent bottle of water and a 5 dollar bottle of water is oftentimes negligible. Lead plumbing is still a problem in some places, as became evident in the high profile case of <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">Flint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, but hotels and public water dispensers are unlikely to be affected.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease">Disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=91" title="Edit section: Disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Being a highly industrialized nation, the United States is largely free from most serious communicable diseases found in many developing nations; however, the <b>HIV rate is higher</b> than in Canada and Western Europe, with about a 0.5% infection rate in the overall population.
</p><p>For the latest in traveler's health information pertaining to the United States, including advisories and recommendations, visit the <b>Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</b> (<b>CDC</b>) website for <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/destinations/traveler/none/united-states">health information for travellers to the United States</a>.
</p><p>There are two <a href="/wiki/Infectious_diseases" title="Infectious diseases">infectious diseases</a> that are worth becoming educated about:
</p>
<ul><li>Human cases of <b>rabies</b> are quite rare in the United States, though the disease is more prevalent in eastern regions of the country. Rabies is usually contracted from <b>animal bites</b> or saliva. <b>If you are bitten by any mammal,</b> even if it's "just a scratch", see a doctor as soon as possible. <b>If you wait until you get symptoms of rabies, you are almost certain to die;</b> if you get the vaccine before symptoms occur, you have a high chance of surviving. <b>Bats</b> and other small, wild animals are especially prone to carry the rabies virus. If you happen to find a bat in the room (particularly upon waking up, or in the room of an unattended child), call or see a doctor since there may have been an unnoticed bite. Avoid other wild mammals like raccoons, skunks, and foxes, even if they seem tame and approachable.</li>
<li><b>Lyme disease</b> is spread via the <a href="/wiki/Ticks" class="mw-redirect" title="Ticks">deer tick</a>, which is prevalent in the woodlands and open fields of many rural areas. There have been cases of Lyme disease in every state, but the great majority have been reported in the Northeast, Mid-Atlantic states and Great Lakes states such as <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>. When venturing into the outdoors, it is a good idea to apply an <b>insect repellent</b> onto exposed skin surfaces that is effective against deer ticks. Should you get flu-like symptoms after hiking through wooded areas, make sure to get tested for Lyme disease, as it is often confused with other diseases, and early treatment is usually quite effective.</li></ul>
<p>Other diseases that are endemic within the United States, but are of far less concern, include <b>Hantaviral Pulmonary Syndrome</b> (found in western regions), <b>Rocky Mountain Spotted Fever</b> (mostly in the Rocky Mountain region), <b>West Nile Virus</b> (all regions) and <b>Eastern/Western Equine Encephalitis</b> (particularly in the Midwest).
</p><p>Due to the high amount of travel and the fact that diaspora communities from almost every country in the world have some presence here., the U.S. is somewhat more likely than other places to have "imported" cases of pandemics, as seen in the case of the Ebola epidemic of 2014. Again this is unlikely to be of concern to you.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_care">Health care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=92" title="Edit section: Health care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>American health care is generally first-class, but very expensive. <b>Make sure that your <a href="/wiki/Travel_insurance" title="Travel insurance">travel insurance</a> is valid for the U.S.</b> Given the high costs, some "world-wide" insurance specifically does not cover the U.S. But if you can afford it, the U.S. is by far the world's leading nation in medical research, and you have at your disposal the most cutting edge treatments that are often not available anywhere else. Long-term visitors to the U.S. (e.g., work or student visa holders) are generally required to take up private health insurance as part of their visa conditions. If you are planning to work in the U.S., check with your employer to see if health insurance is provided as part of your employee benefits.  
</p><p>To the patient, America's public (20%), private for-profit (20%), and private not-for-profit (60%) hospitals are generally indistinguishable. Inner city public hospitals may be more crowded and less well maintained, but as a whole both costs and service levels are consistently high in all types. <b>No hospital can refuse a life-threatening emergency case.</b> Private hospitals may only stabilize such patients before sending them to a nearby public hospital, which will generally act as the regional center for 24-hour emergency treatment.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Med_015_(8185755069).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Med_015_%288185755069%29.jpg/220px-Med_015_%288185755069%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Med_015_%288185755069%29.jpg/330px-Med_015_%288185755069%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Med_015_%288185755069%29.jpg/440px-Med_015_%288185755069%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Med_015_(8185755069).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ambulance in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a></div></div></div>
<p>In a life-threatening emergency, call <b>911</b> to summon an ambulance to take you to the nearest <b>hospital emergency room</b> ("<b>ER</b>"), or in less urgent situations get to the hospital yourself and register at the ER's front desk. Ambulance fees typically range from a few hundred to a few thousand dollars, and though they will never refuse to transport you in an emergency, the ambulance fees will be billed to you at a later date. Emergency rooms treat patients regardless of their ability to pay, even though their services are <b>not free</b>. Expect to pay at least $500 for a visit, plus the cost of any specific services or medications you are given. <b>Avoid</b> using ERs for non-emergency walk-in care; they are 3–4 times more expensive than other options and your non-urgent condition means you will have a wait of hours or maybe days. Most urban areas also have minor emergency centers (also called "urgent care", etc.) for conditions that don't require a visit to the emergency room (e.g. superficial lacerations). Their hours may be limited; few are open at night.
</p><p>Walk-in <b>clinics</b> can provide routine medical care; to find one, check the yellow pages (see <a href="#By_phone">By phone</a> below) under "Clinics", or call a major hospital and ask. Patients see a doctor or nurse practitioner without an appointment (but often with a bit of a wait). They are typically very up-front about fees, and always accept credit cards. Make sure the clerk knows you will be paying "out of pocket"; if they assume an insurance company is paying, they may inflate the bill with unnecessary extras.
</p><p><b>Dentists</b> are accustomed to explaining fees over the phone, and most will accept credit cards. Health insurance typically does <i>not</i> provide dental coverage; you will need to take up separate <b>dental insurance</b> for that.
</p><p>Government-supported clinics offering free or low-cost testing and treatment for <b>sexually transmitted diseases</b> are widespread. Local Health Departments will provide more details. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.plannedparenthood.org/">Planned Parenthood</a> (1-800-230-7526) is a private agency with clinics and centers around the country providing birth control and other reproductive health services.
</p><p>Note the difference between a red cross and a green cross: in the United States, anything medically related will have a red cross, whereas medical marijuana dispensaries will have a green cross.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cope">Cope</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=93" title="Edit section: Cope">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_services">Religious services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=94" title="Edit section: Religious services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christian visitors looking to attend religious services should have no problem locating a house of worship, even in small towns. A typical medium-sized American town or city probably has one or more Catholic parishes, several Protestant churches (the most common being Baptist, Pentecostal, Lutheran, Presbyterian, Methodist and Episcopal/Anglican), and possibly other houses of worship depending on the demographics of the area (such as synagogues or mosques).
</p><p>Most churches in the United States practice an "open table", meaning they welcome you to participate in worship, and some or all rituals, even if you're not a member of their religious denomination.  Some churches, and some entire denominations, welcome LGBT individuals.
</p><p>Some churches also have after-church luncheon for free or at a nominal cost. Visitors are always welcome to stay for lunch and fellowship as a way to meet locals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="News_and_media">News and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=95" title="Edit section: News and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Though it's not as ubiquitous as before the advent of the Internet, print media isn't dead yet. Just about every mid-sized city (and many small ones) has a daily newspaper covering local news and often some national news. Major metropolitan areas will usually have more than one paper to choose from. With a few exceptions (mostly tabloids like the <i>New York Post</i> and <i>New York Daily News</i>), most papers provide reasonably balanced coverage of hard news, with their political biases manifesting themselves only in their editorial or opinion sections.
</p><p>The national paper of record is <i><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/">The New York Times</a></b></i> ($2.50 daily, $6 Sunday); its coverage of national and international issues makes it daily reading just about anywhere in the country.  For financial news, <i><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.wsj.com/">The Wall Street Journal</a></b></i> (also based in NYC, $2) is similarly well-respected and widely read.  For a more casual but still informative format, <i><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.usatoday.com/">USA Today</a></b></i> ($2) is published five days a week; it's the most widely circulated print newspaper in the country.  Many hotels offer free copies of either the local paper or <i>USA Today</i>; ask at the front desk.  Other widely read papers include the <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.latimes.com/">Los Angeles Times</a></i> (known for its West Coast coverage) and <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/">The Washington Post</a></i> (with exemplary political reporting from the nation's capital). <i><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.time.com/">Time</a></b></i> and <i><b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.newsweek.com/">Newsweek</a></b></i> are newsmagazines published weekly that offers more in-depth feature coverage.
</p><p>Major metropolitan areas also have a full suite of <b>broadcast television</b> stations; small cities might have only two or three local stations, especially if they're within broadcast range of a larger city. The major broadcast networks are <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://abc.go.com/">ABC</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cbs.com/">CBS</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nbc.com/">NBC</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.fox.com/">Fox</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pbs.com/">PBS</a> (taxpayer-subsidized public broadcasting); each has local stations in each market that broadcast local and national news, syndicated shows, and in-house TV series. Almost the entire country is wired for cable TV. That opens up a whole range of options for viewing, from <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cnn.com/">CNN</a></b> for news to <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://weather.com/">The Weather Channel</a></b> to <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.espn.com/">ESPN</a></b> for sports, along with countless entertainment channels. 
</p><p><b>Broadcast radio</b> is a much more fragmented market than television; major cities have dozens of stations on both AM and FM bands. The AM band is mostly used for talk formats; music stations are almost exclusively found on the FM band.  Many rental cars come equipped with <b>satellite radio</b> from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.siriusxm.com/">SiriusXM</a>, which offers hundreds of channels of music, comedy, news, talk, and sports, without the need to keep finding new stations as you drive across the country.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Respect">Respect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=96" title="Edit section: Respect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg/250px-Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg/375px-Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg/500px-Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1457" data-file-height="1823" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_Man_2011_Victor_Grigas_The_man_IMG_4602_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Black_Rock_City" title="Black Rock City">Burning Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a></div></div></div>
<p>The United States is a very diverse country, meaning that <b>cultural norms can vary significantly</b> from region to region, and it is difficult to generalize what could be offensive and what could not. For instance, while making homophobic statements would be very offensive in a liberal area like New York City, the opposite could be true in a strongly Evangelical rural town in the South.
</p>
<ul><li>It is polite to firmly <b>shake hands</b> when meeting someone or being introduced, and when concluding a business meeting or departing for a long time, though handshaking is often skipped in less formal situations. In casual situations, some people may offer a fist bump, a more complicated handshake or even a hug. Just follow along; mistakes in those situations are no big deal at all. Kissing on the cheeks in greeting is rare and usually done only between close friends and family.</li>
<li><p>As an adult, once you're introduced to someone, you can usually call them by their <b>first name</b>. If someone gives <i>only</i> their first name when being introduced, you can definitely call them that way. Calling someone by their last name is more formal, and with rare exceptions (e.g. sports teams) is always done with "Mr./Mrs./Ms./Miss", or with a professional title (e.g., "Doctor <i>[last name]</i>" or "Professor <i>[last name]</i>" or "Officer <i>[last name]</i>"). Such professional titles can also be used alone without a name ("Doctor" [only for a medical doctor] or "Professor" or "Officer"), but not so with "waiter", "bus/taxi driver", "flight attendant", etc., which are jobs, not titles. If you don't know someone's name, use "sir/ma'am".</p><p>If you're still not certain, and there are no locals around to set an example, it's safer to be overly polite and use last names. Many people will soon respond with "Please, call me <i>[first name]</i>". Alternatively, you can ask someone how they would like to be addressed.</p>
<ul><li><p>Students should call teachers "Professor <i>[last name]</i>" or "Professor" (at college level), or "Dr. <i>[last name]</i>" or "Mr./Mrs./Ms./Miss <i>[last name]</i>" (at any level) depending on whether the teacher has a doctoral degree. "Instructor" and "teacher" are jobs, not titles, so you can talk about "my instructor" or "your teacher", but should not call someone "Instructor" or "Teacher". That being said, it is fairly common for PhD students to address their supervisors by first names; when in doubt, just follow the example of your fellow students.</p><p>There's no set way to address a college TA (teaching assistant); calling them "Professor" is totally inaccurate, and "teaching assistant" is not a title. Sticking with "Mr./Ms." is a safe option, although since TAs are fellow students, most will accept or prefer first names.</p></li>
<li>At a homestay, a safe choice is to call your homestay parents "Mr./Mrs. <i>[last name]</i>" (in this instance, you don't need to use "Dr." in place of "Mr./Mrs."); in most of the country, that's what their children's friends would normally call them. Depending on the family, they may ask you to call them by their first names, or even to call them "mom" and "dad".</li></ul></li>
<li>Unless it is really crowded, leave about an arm's length of <b>personal space</b> between yourself and others.</li>
<li><b>Punctuality</b> is valued: being five minutes late is not usually a problem, but if you will be any later, try to call ahead in order to give a warning.</li>
<li>Americans often draw a strong distinction between their <b>work</b> and <b>personal life</b>. As a general rule, it is inappropriate to ask people more than superficial details about their family and other aspects of their personal life.</li>
<li>As a result of the country's history of racial discrimination and the modern push toward equality, Americans are exceptionally touchy about issues of <b>race</b>. If you have to reference race, <i>Black</i> or <i>African-American</i>,  <i>Asian</i>, <i>Latino</i> or <i>Hispanic</i>, <i>Native American</i> or <i>American Indian</i>, <i>Pacific Islander</i>, and <i>White</i> or <i>Caucasian</i> are acceptable terms.
<ul><li>There are some racist tropes that have historically appeared in caricatures, and it is best to avoid alluding to any of them in normal conversation. A lot of this is probably common sense, but there are a few that might not be obvious to some foreign visitors to the United States. For example, African-Americans have been caricatured in print and film countless times eating fried chicken and watermelon, so tread carefully when asking an African-American where to get these.</li>
<li>Do not tell jokes about race or ethnicity; some of these may be told casually in many other countries, but they are very likely to cause offense in the U.S.</li>
<li>Avoid showing or talking about Confederate symbols, especially Confederate flags or the song "Dixie". Although regarded by some as an integral part of Southern identity, these are controversial throughout most of the country and increasingly associated with white supremacy, racism and other negative stereotypes about the South.</li>
<li>The <b>Swastika</b> symbol is very offensive in the U.S. owing to its association with anti-Semitism, Nazism and white supremacy. You should avoid displaying the symbol, even for religious reasons.</li></ul></li>
<li><b>Gender</b> and <b>sexuality</b> are also sensitive issues and best avoided as conversation topics with people you don't know well. As with race, jokes about these are also very likely to offend and best avoided.</li>
<li>There are <b>Indian reservations</b> scattered throughout the country. Many of these reservations are home to sites that are sacred to the tribe, and certain places may be off-limits to all but tribe members. If you enter a reservation, respect its land and people.</li>
<li><b>Gun control</b> is a very polarizing and sensitive issue. Visitors (particularly to rural areas, which tend toward vehement opposition to any legal restrictions on firearm ownership whatsoever) should avoid this topic of conversation if at all possible, and tread very lightly otherwise.</li>
<li>Since at least the 1990s, the trend has been for Americans to become increasingly polarized in their political beliefs, and nowadays, political allegiance can take precedence over reason and civility. If it even sounds like you are saying something positive about a political figure or policy that someone is against (or speaking ill of a figure or policy they support), you may receive some verbal hostility. However, physically violent reactions to political statements remain rare.</li></ul>
<p>Also see the section on <a href="#Tipping">tipping</a>, and the section on <a href="#Smoking">smoking</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dress">Dress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=97" title="Edit section: Dress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Clothing" class="mw-redirect" title="Clothing">Dress</a> in the U.S. tends to be fairly <b>casual</b>. For everyday clothes, jeans and T-shirts are always acceptable, as are shorts when the weather is suitable. Sneakers (athletic shoes) are common; flip-flops and sandals are also popular in warm weather. In the winter seasons in northern states boots are commonly worn.
</p><p>In general, dress code is a bit more formal in the metropolises of the Atlantic, and in the South (where "Southern style" is a surviving concept), and more relaxed the further west you go.
</p><p>Generally, Americans accept religious attire such as yarmulkes, hijabs and burqas without comment.
</p><p>At the workplace, <b>business casual</b> (slacks, understated collared shirts without a tie, and non-athletic shoes) is now the default at many companies. More traditional industries (e.g. finance, legal, and insurance) still require suits and ties. Other industries (e.g. computer software) are even more casual, allowing jeans and even shorts for everyday wear; as a business visitor, a safe choice would be business casual, or jeans and a collared shirt.
</p><p>When dressing up for nice restaurants or upscale entertainment, a pair of nice slacks, a collared shirt, and dress shoes will work almost everywhere. Ties for men are rarely necessary, but jackets are occasionally required for very upscale restaurants in big cities (such restaurants will almost always have jackets to lend).
</p><p>At the beach or pool, men prefer loose bathing trunks or boardshorts, and women wear bikinis or one-piece swimsuits. Nude bathing is not generally acceptable and is usually illegal except at certain private beaches or resorts; even women going topless is not usually accepted by most people, and is also illegal in some states.
</p><p><b>Breastfeeding in public</b> can be a touchy subject. While most mothers in the U.S. do breastfeed at home for at least a little while, a woman doing so in public, whether covered or not, can elicit complaints about indecent exposure, often from other mothers. All states/territories except Idaho and Puerto Rico have laws explicitly allowing women to breastfeed in public, and 29 states plus D.C. and the Virgin Islands also exempt breastfeeding from prosecution for public indecency or indecent exposure; some businesses have also changed their policies in the last few years to allow and protect breastfeeding mothers. However, public attitudes vary, and a mother breastfeeding in public, particularly uncovered, may receive some unwanted stares and in rare cases negative comments.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Connect">Connect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=98" title="Edit section: Connect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_phone">By phone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=99" title="Edit section: By phone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG/220px-Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG/330px-Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG/440px-Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phone_charging_station_at_Newark_airport.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phone charging station at <a href="/wiki/Newark_airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Newark airport">Newark airport</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_calls">Domestic calls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=100" title="Edit section: Domestic calls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <b>country code</b> for the U.S. is <b>+1</b>. The long-distance prefix (trunk code) is also "1", so U.S. telephone numbers are frequently written as an eleven-digit number: "1-nnn-nnn-nnnn". The rest of the telephone number consists of ten digits: a three-digit <b>area code</b>, and a seven-digit number. There can be many area codes in large cities, and only one or two for the entirety of a mostly rural state. The area code does not indicate whether a number is a mobile or a landline. Also, many Americans do not change their numbers when they move, so the area code of their mobile number may not match landlines in the region.
</p><p><b>From a mobile phone</b>, a domestic call is simple: always dial ten digits without the "1".
</p><p><b>From a fixed line</b>, you can usually dial a local number using ten digits. <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> require eleven digits. Regions with only one area code usually allow seven digits. If a number is written or given without the area code, you can usually dial it like that locally, but dialing ten digits also works. For long-distance and toll-free, always dial eleven digits.
</p><p>Domestic calls to area codes 800, 888, 877, 866, 855, 844 and 833 are <b>toll-free</b>. From a fixed line, they must be dialed using the full 11-digit pattern. With few exceptions (such as Canada or, rarely, Mexico) these are not reachable from abroad. (<a href="/wiki/Internet_telephony" title="Internet telephony">VoIP</a> and Skype users may be able to circumvent this restriction by calling via a U.S.-based gateway.)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_calls">International calls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=101" title="Edit section: International calls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>To dial abroad from the U.S., the <b>international access code</b> is <b>011</b>. On a mobile phone, "+" will also work.
</p><p>Canada, U.S. territories, Bermuda, and 17 Caribbean nations are part of the <b>North American Numbering Plan</b>, and have the same +1 country code as the U.S. Calls made between these countries are dialed using only the full 11-digit number, without the "011" or "+" access code, but almost all are charged at international rates. Calls between the U.S. and its territories may be more expensive than calls within the contiguous 48 states and D.C., or even calls between the U.S. mainland and Canada (which are typically charged at a higher rate than domestic calls, but lower than other international calls). Alaska and Hawaii may carry a surcharge even for domestic calls, depending on the carrier and rate plan.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phones_and_directories">Phones and directories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=102" title="Edit section: Phones and directories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Pay phones</b> can be hard to find. Likely locations include in or near stores and restaurants, shopping mall entrances and near bus stops. Most are coin operated (quarters, dimes and nickels) and do not accept banknotes.  Prices are normally $0.50 for the first three minutes, and $0.25 for each additional minute. An online directory of pay phones can be found at <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.payphone-directory.org/">Pay Phone Directory</a>.  Calls to 911 (to report an emergency) and to toll-free area codes (800, 888, 877, 866, 855, 844 and 833) are free from pay phones. A few commercial toll-free numbers block inbound calls from U.S. payphones.
</p><p>Telephone directories are often split into two books: the <b>white pages</b> list phone numbers alphabetically by last name, and the <b>yellow pages</b> list businesses by category (e.g. "Taxicabs"). Many residential land-line phones and all mobile phones are unlisted. <b>Directory assistance</b> can be had (at an extra cost) by dialing <b>411</b> (for local numbers) or <b>1-<i>[area code]</i>-555-1212</b> (for other areas). If 411 doesn't work, try 555-1212, <i>[area code]</i>-555-1212 or 1-<i>[area code]</i>-555-1212. <b>Free directory information</b> (with advertisements) is available: dial 1-800-FREE-411 (1-800-3733-411) or browse <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://free411.com">free411.com</a> or <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://411.info">411.info</a>. Regional telephone companies' web sites (most often AT&amp;T, Verizon, or CenturyLink) also provide directory information. Using the website of the company that operates in the region you are interested in yields the best results.
</p><p>Long-distance telephone <b>calling cards</b> are available at most convenience stores. They are generally aimed at specific types of call (e.g. domestic, or to particular countries). Credit can often be replenished over the phone using a credit or debit card, but foreign bank cards may be refused. Card calls from payphones via toll-free numbers printed on the cards may be more expensive. There may also be effective charges per connection as well as per minute; some cards also carry hidden weekly or monthly charges which deplete their value.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_phones">Mobile phones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=103" title="Edit section: Mobile phones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The four largest mobile phone networks in the U.S. are <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.att.com/shop/wireless">AT&amp;T</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.verizonwireless.com/">Verizon Wireless</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sprint.com/">Sprint</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.t-mobile.com/">T-Mobile</a>. These all have good coverage of practically all urban/suburban and many rural areas of the country, although each network has strong and weak areas.
</p><p>There is no surcharge for calling to a mobile phone (calls to mobile phones are charged the same as calls to land lines), and mobile phones don't pay surcharges when calling domestic long-distance. Instead, <b>mobile phones themselves are charged for all usage, outgoing and incoming</b>. In other words, a call to/from a mobile phone carries the same cost <i>to that mobile phone</i>, but it doesn't matter whether it's local, domestic long-distance, or toll-free. Credit packages from $25/month allow you to make hundreds of minutes' worth of calls. A failed call (or a "missed call") will be charged since you are billed from the moment you dial.
</p><p>If you want to have a mobile phone in the U.S. while you travel, you have several options:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Using your phone from home</b> isn't as easy as in some other countries, because North America and the western half of South America use the 850 and 1900&#160;MHz frequencies, instead of 900 and 1800&#160;MHz used elsewhere. If you have a phone from the same region or a phone that's <b>tri- or quad-band</b> (which includes many modern phones), you should be fine; otherwise, this option won't work for you. You will also need to pay attention to whether your phone is GSM/UMTS (used by AT&amp;T and T-Mobile; common in Europe) or CDMA (used by Verizon and Sprint). However, all four operators use the same 4G LTE standard that is used internationally.
<ul><li><b>Roaming service</b> (using your home phone number by simply calling through a U.S. network) is expensive, and will depend on the networks your home provider has contracts with, as well as your own provider's fees. <b>Internet data plans</b> are ubiquitous in the U.S., but the normally-high prices become exorbitant once roaming fees are added.
<ul><li>Canadian cell phones may roam at C$1.50/minute or more, although plans vary; prepaid-cash users may not roam at all. Freedom Mobile offers a good package.</li></ul></li>
<li><p><b>Buying a SIM card</b> is a better way to use your personal phone; by installing the SIM card in your phone, you'll have a local U.S. telephone number prepaid with no contract, hundreds of minutes' worth of calls, and large amounts of data. The prices make it more economical for extended stays, but the convenience of cheap calls and data make this an attractive option for any visitor.</p><p>SIM cards are available for purchase at most electronics stores, as well as some "big-box" retailers including Wal-Mart and Target. Make sure that your phone is not SIM-locked and is compatible with the SIM card and the frequencies of the network. Read the terms carefully, as some plans are recurring monthly contracts rather than one-time prepaid plans.</p><p>Providers who sell prepaid SIM cards include <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.att.com/shop/wireless/gophone.html">AT&amp;T's GoPhone</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mycricket.com/">Cricket</a> (which is owned by AT&amp;T), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.straighttalkbyop.com/">Straight Talk's Bring Your Own Phone</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://prepaid-phones.t-mobile.com/phone-sim-card">T-Mobile</a>.</p></li></ul></li>
<li><b>Purchasing prepaid minutes and a basic mobile phone</b> is your next best option. These can be found at some grocery stores, at most electronics, office supply, and convenience stores, and of course online. A basic phone (without Internet access) and 60–100 minutes of time can be purchased for under $50. In addition to minutes, some prepaid services charge a flat fee per month (e.g. $20/month), or a fee for days when the phone is actually used (e.g. $1.25/day). Prepaid, contract-free mobile phone service is available from many prepaid-only providers, such as <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.boostmobile.com/">Boost Mobile</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mycricket.com/">Cricket</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.straighttalk.com/">Straight Talk</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tracfone.com/">TracFone</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.virginmobileusa.com/">Virgin Mobile USA</a>, as well as limited offerings from the major carriers: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.att.com/shop/wireless/gophone.html">AT&amp;T's GoPhone</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://prepaid-phones.t-mobile.com/">T-Mobile</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.verizonwireless.com/wcms/consumer/shop/prepaid.html">Verizon Prepaid Wireless</a>.</li>
<li><b>Renting a phone</b> costs from around $3/day, and can be done at shops in most of the larger airports. Depending on how long you're staying and how much you plan on calling or using data, it may be cheaper or easier to use a prepaid SIM card or prepaid phone instead.</li>
<li><b>Getting a phone contract</b> may make sense if you are planning to stay longer. Unless you've lived in the U.S. for several months, you won't have a credit rating that is recognized by U.S. service providers and won't be able to subscribe to these plans (although some providers will let you get one with a deposit, typically at least $500). Contracts typically require a 24-month commitment (cancellation fees can reach $300!) to a monthly rate plan, and in exchange they subsidize the cost of the phone (so basic phones are "free", and smartphones only "cost" $50&#8211;$200).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_mail">By mail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=104" title="Edit section: By mail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo,_Utah.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo%2C_Utah.JPG/220px-Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo%2C_Utah.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo%2C_Utah.JPG/330px-Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo%2C_Utah.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo%2C_Utah.JPG/440px-Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo%2C_Utah.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Self_Service_center_at_US_Post_Office_in_Provo,_Utah.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Self Service Postal Center</div></div></div>
<p>Addressing mail with a properly-formatted address will expedite its journey with the <b><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.usps.com/">United States Postal Service</a></b> (USPS, not to be confused with the private shipper UPS). The <b>ZIP code</b> (postal code) is important, and you can <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.usps.com/zip4/">look up ZIP codes and correct address formats</a> online. A 5-digit ZIP code identifies a main post office; a 4-digit extension (recommended but optional) may narrow this to one business or an individual building. Addresses should be written in three to four lines with the name of recipient, house number and street name, suite or apartment or building number, city or town, two-letter state abbreviation, ZIP code, e.g.:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Wikimedia Foundation, Inc.<br />149 New Montgomery St.<br />San Francisco, CA 94105-3739</p></blockquote>
<p>First-class international airmail postcards and letters (up to one ounce/28.5 grams) cost $1.15. All locations with a ZIP code are considered domestic, including the 50 states, U.S. possessions, <a href="/wiki/Federated_States_of_Micronesia" title="Federated States of Micronesia">Micronesia (FSM)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">Marshall Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a> and overseas military bases, ships (APO or FPO) and diplomatic posts (APO or DPO). Domestic postcards cost $0.35, and ordinary letters up to an ounce, $0.55 (2019). "Forever" stamps are available for the first ounce of domestic and international postage, and protect against future increases. Mailing thick or rigid objects, or non-standard shapes increases the postage cost.
</p><p><b>Poste Restante</b>, the receiving of mail at a post office rather than a private address, is called <b>General Delivery</b>. There is no charge for this service. You must show ID such as a passport to pick up your mail. Post offices will usually hold mail for up to 30 days. If the city is large enough to have multiple post offices, <b>only one</b> (usually in the center of downtown) will allow General Delivery. 
</p><p>FedEx and UPS also have a "Hold for Pickup" option and have locations throughout larger cities in the U.S. Though usually more expensive, these may be a better option when receiving something important from abroad.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_Internet">By Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=105" title="Edit section: By Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Given the ubiquity of private Internet access, Internet cafés are <i>rare</i> outside major cities and tourist areas. However, you do have some options, except perhaps in the most rural of areas. Accessible Wi-Fi networks, however, are common.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wireless">Wireless</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=106" title="Edit section: Wireless">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The most generally useful Wi-Fi spots are in <b>coffee shops, fast-food chains, and bookshops</b>, though you may need to buy something first. Some cities also provide free Wi-Fi across their downtown areas. Try to use only public networks. Using a private network (even one without a password), unless authorized to do so, is illegal (though enforcement is nearly non-existent), and it may also allow criminals to track your browsing and so defraud you. Even traffic on public networks may be logged.
</p><p>A few less obvious Wi-Fi spots may be found in:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Public libraries</b> – Free Wi-Fi is almost always available, although you may need to get a log-in from the information desk. The network may even be accessible 24/7, so even if the library is closed you may be able to sit outside and surf.</li>
<li><b>Hotels</b>  – chain hotels usually have it in the rooms and the communal areas; smaller independent hotels vary. An overpriced option at high-end hotels, but included standard at most economy limited service chains.</li>
<li><b>Colleges and universities</b> – may have networks in their libraries and student centers that are open to non-students. Some have networks accessible throughout campus, even outdoors.</li>
<li><b>Airports</b> – even smaller regional ones offer Wi-Fi. They often cost money though. Airport lounges typically provide unlimited free Wi-Fi.</li>
<li><b>Paid Wi-Fi chains</b> – give you access to numerous hotspots for a small charge, e.g. Boingo.</li></ul>
<p><b>Mobile broadband</b> via a USB modem is also an option. Service providers include Verizon Wireless and Virgin Mobile (which uses the Sprint network). Make sure to check a coverage map before you buy, each company has large areas with bad or no coverage. Also, these plans are subject to data limits which are easy to exceed unknowingly! Avoid watching videos over a mobile network.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_PC_terminals">Public PC terminals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=107" title="Edit section: Public PC terminals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Internet cafés</b> can still be found in some larger cities. Airports and shopping malls offer Internet access terminals for quick use, although these are generally disappearing. Access typically costs $1 for 1–2 minutes of web time. Any public computer will likely block access to undesirable websites.
</p><p>You may also consider:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Public libraries</b> — have PCs with broadband for public use, but you may need a library card. Some libraries give out free internet cards that have no book-lending privileges for out-of-area visitors.</li>
<li><b>Photocopy shops</b> —  will have computers available for public use (at a cost), e.g. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.fedex.com/us/office/indexnew.html">FedEx Office</a> (<abbr title="phone" style="text-decoration:none">☏</abbr> <bdi class="tel listing-phone"><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="tel:+1-800-4633339">+1-800-GOFEDEX (4633339)</a></span></bdi>; when prompted by the voice menu, say "FedEx Office" or press "64") is open 24&#160;hr and is nationwide. Some are also commercial mail receiving agents (such as The UPS Store) and offer fax service.</li>
<li><b>Smart hotels</b>  — have "business centers" replete with computers, printers, photocopies, and fax machines.</li>
<li><b>Electronics stores</b> — the computers on display are often connected to the Internet. A quick email will be tolerated with a smile; six hours of Warcraft won't. The Apple Store is particularly generous and will allow browsing without intent to buy; however, some websites, such as Facebook, are blocked.</li>
<li><b>University libraries</b> — while private universities may restrict entry to their students and faculty, public university libraries are generally required by law to be open to the public  and they may also have a computer or two for public use.</li></ul>
